The Dialectical Analysis of Hidaya: Definition, Objection, and Resolution
Commentator dialectically examines two definitions of hidaya, uses Qur'anic verses as objections, and resolves via homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical argument about hidaya: identify its two definitions, the objections from the Qur'an, and the resolution via homonymy.

Core Idea
Hidaya is a homonym whose two meanings resolve apparent Quranic contradictions.

The word 'hidaya' (guidance) appears in the Qur'an in ways that seem contradictory. The commentator examines two definitions, tests them against verses, and then resolves the tension by showing that 'hidaya' has two distinct meanings based on grammatical usage. This lesson walks you through that dialectical process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposed definitions of hidaya
Find in the Arabic the two definitions: (1) al-dalala al-musila (the indication that leads to the goal) and (2) ira'at al-tariq (showing the way that leads to the goal).
2
Understand the logical difference
The first definition entails actually reaching the goal; the second only entails showing the way, not necessarily arrival.
3
Study the first objection
The verse about Thamud (41:17): 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance.' If guidance meant arrival, they could not later be misguided. This refutes the first definition.
4
Study the second objection
The verse about the Prophet (28:56): 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills.' The Prophet's role was to show the way, yet the verse says he cannot guide. This refutes the second definition (since showing the way was his function).
5
Discover the resolution from the author's Hashiya
The author states that hidaya is a homonym (lafz mushtarak). When it takes a direct object (e.g., 'Ihdina al-sirata al-mustaqim'), it means delivering (isal). When it takes an object with 'ila' or 'lam' (e.g., 'yahdi ila siratin mustaqim'), it means showing the way (irat al-tariq). Thus both definitions are valid in different contexts, and the objections are repelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «الذي هدانا» الهداية (۲۲) قيل: هي (۲۳) الدلالة الموصلة اى الايصال (۲۴) الى المطلوب وقيل : هی (۲۵) ارائة الطريق (۲۶) الموصل الى المطلوب. والفرق بين هذين المعنيين ان الأول يستلزم الوصول الى المطلوب بخلاف الثاني فان الدلالة على ما يوصل الى المطلوب لا يلزم ان تكون موصلة الى ما يوصل فكيف توصل الى المطلوب ؟ والاول منقوض بقوله تعالى: «و اما نمود فهديناهم فاستحبوا العمى على الهدى» (۲۷) اذلايتصور الضلال بعد الوصول الى الحق (۲۸). والثانى منقوض بقوله تعالى : انك لا تهدى من احببت ولكن الله يهدى من يشاء» (۲۹) فان النبي (ص) كان شأنه وانة الطريق (٣٠)) والذى يفهم من كلام المصنف فى حاشية الكشاف (۳۱) هو ان الهداية لفظ مشترك بين هذين المعنيين و ح (۳۲) يظهر اندفاع كلا النقضين و يرتفع الخلاف من البين. و محصول كلام المصنف فى تنك الحاشية: ان الهداية لفظ يتعدى الى المفعول الثاني تارة بنفسه نحو: «أهدنا الصراط المستقيم » (۳۳) وتارة بـ «الى» نحو: «والله يهدى من يشاء الى صراط مستقيم وتارة باللام (۳۴) نحو: «ان هذا القرآن يهدى للتي هي اقوم) فمعناها على الاستعمال الأول هو الايصال وعلى الثانى (۳۵) اراثة الطريق.
He said, 'Who guided us': Guidance (al-hidaya) — it is said: it is the guiding indication that leads, i.e., delivering to the desired goal. And it is said: it is showing the way that leads to the desired goal. The difference between these two meanings is that the first entails reaching the desired goal, unlike the second, for indicating what leads to the desired goal does not necessarily entail being that which leads to what leads, so how could it lead to the desired goal? The first is refuted by His saying, 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance' (41:17), for misguidance is inconceivable after reaching the truth. The second is refuted by His saying, 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills' (28:56), for the Prophet (s) was concerned with showing the way. And what is understood from the author's statement in his Hashiya on al-Kashshaf is that 'guidance' is a homonym between these two meanings, and then it appears that both refutations are repelled and the disagreement disappears. And the summary of the author's statement in that Hashiya: that the word 'guidance' takes its second object sometimes by itself, as in 'Guide us to the straight path' (1:6), and sometimes with 'to' (ila), as in 'And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path' (2:213), and sometimes with the lam, as in 'Indeed, this Qur'an guides to that which is most upright' (17:9). So its meaning in the first usage is delivering (isal), and in the second it is showing the way (irat al-tariq).
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical examination of the word 'hidaya' (guidance) as used in the phrase 'الذي هدانا' (Who guided us). The commentator presents two conflicting definitions: one is 'al-dalala al-musila' (the guiding indication that leads to the goal), which entails actual arrival; the other is 'ira'at al-tariq' (showing the way), which does not ensure arrival. Each definition is then tested against Qur'anic verses. The first definition is challenged by the verse about Thamud: God guided them, yet they preferred blindness. If guidance meant actual arrival, they could not later be misguided. The second definition is challenged by the verse telling the Prophet that he cannot guide whom he loves, yet his role was to show the way. The commentator then cites the author's own Hashiya on al-Kashshaf, which resolves the issue by declaring 'hidaya' a homonym (lafz mushtarak) between the two meanings. Furthermore, the author notes that the word takes its second object in three ways: by itself (e.g., 'Ihdina al-sirata al-mustaqim'), with 'ila' (e.g., 'wa Allahu yahdi man yasha'u ila siratin mustaqim'), and with 'lam' (e.g., 'inna hadha al-Qur'ana yahdi lillati hiya aqwam'). The first usage indicates delivering (isal), while the second and third indicate showing the way (irat al-tariq). Thus, both meanings are valid and context-dependent, and the apparent contradictions in the verses are resolved.

Key Terms
الهداية
الدلالة الموصلة
ارائة الطريق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse is used to refute the definition of hidaya as 'delivering to the goal'?
A) 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills.' (28:56)
B) 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance.' (41:17)
C) 'Guide us to the straight path.' (1:6)
D) 'Indeed, this Qur'an guides to that which is most upright.' (17:9)

Key Takeaway
Hidaya is a homonym; its two meanings—delivering and showing the way—are distinguished by grammatical usage, resolving apparent Quranic contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'iman' (faith). Some define it as 'belief in the heart' (tasdiq bil-qalb), others as 'belief plus action' (tasdiq wal-'amal). Find a Qur'anic verse that could challenge the first definition (e.g., 'The believers are only those who, when Allah is mentioned, their hearts tremble...' 8:2, which implies action) and another that could challenge the second definition (e.g., 'And whoever does good of the males or females while he is a believer...' 16:97, which distinguishes belief from deeds). Then propose a resolution using the homonymy technique from this lesson, suggesting that 'iman' may have multiple meanings depending on context.
?
In a debate, argue that hidaya means only 'showing the way' (ira'at al-tariq). Have your opponent object using the Thamud verse (41:17) indicating that they were guided yet went astray. Respond by distinguishing the two meanings as the author did: the verse uses hidaya in the sense of 'showing the way', not 'delivering'.

Analyzing the Definition of 'Sawa' al-Tariq' and the Syntax of 'Ja'ala Lana'
Commentator defines 'sawa' al-tariq' as the middle way and analyzes the syntactic attachment of 'lana'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the commentator's definition of 'sawa' al-tariq', distinguish its literal and metaphorical meanings, explain the preference for the general interpretation, and identify the two possible syntactic attachments of the adverb 'lana'.

Core Idea
A single phrase can carry multiple layers of meaning—literal, metaphorical, and contextual—demanding careful analysis.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks two key phrases from the original text: 'sawa' al-tariq' and 'ja'ala lana'. He shows how a seemingly simple term like 'sawa' al-tariq' can be grounded in literal definition, extended metaphorically, and then situated between two possible interpretations. Similarly, the grammatical attachment of 'lana' offers two readings, each with a different nuance. This lesson will guide you through these analytical steps, highlighting the precision expected in classical logical commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base phrases
Locate the two phrases being explained: 'sawa' al-tariq' and 'ja'ala lana'. Recognize that the commentator is providing a gloss on each.
The phrases are from the original matn (the base text) and are being expanded upon.
2
Analyze the definition of 'sawa' al-tariq'
Notice that the commentator first gives a literal definition: 'its middle' (wasatuhu). Then he says it is a metaphor (kinaya) for the even path and the straight path, which are mutually entailing.
The literal meaning is part of the road, but the metaphorical meaning points to a correct way of life or truth.
3
Examine the two possible interpretations
Understand that the commentator then offers two possible referents for the path: the general state of affairs (nafs al-amr 'umuman) or the specific religion of Islam. He prefers the first because it yields manifest excellence in relation to both parts of the book.
This choice affects how the phrase is understood in context.
4
Parse the syntactic attachment of 'lana'
Determine the two possible grammatical connections of the adverb 'lana': either it is attached to the verb 'ja'ala' with the lam indicating benefit, as in the Qur'anic verse 'wa ja'ala lakumu al-arda firashan', or it is attached to a companion (rafiq) with fronting of the object of the genitive construction.
Each attachment gives a different emphasis: benefit vs. accompaniment.
5
Connect to the broader logical context
Reflect on how this detailed analysis serves to clarify the author's intended meaning and avoid ambiguity, which is crucial in logical and theological discussions.
Precision in language is fundamental to the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « سواء الطريق»: اى وسطه (۳۶) الذي يفضى سالكه الى المطلوب البتة (۳۷) و هذا كناية (۳۸) عن الطريق المستوى (۳۹) والصراط المستقم اذهما متلازمان وهذا مراد من فسّره بالطريق المستوى و الصراط المستقم. ثم المراد به (۴۰) اما نفس الأمر عموماً او خصوص ملة الاسلام (۴۱)، والاول اولى (۴۲) لخحصول البراعة الظاهرة بالقياس الى قسمي الكتاب. قوله جعل لنا» الظرف (۴۳) اما متعلق بجعل واللام للانتفاع كما قيل في قوله تعالى: (وجعل لكم الارض فراشاً» (۴۴) و اما برفيق و يكون تقديم معمول المضاف
He said, 'the straight path' (sawa' al-tariq): i.e., its middle, which leads its traveler inevitably to the desired goal. And this is a metaphor for the even path and the straight path, for they are mutually entailing, and this is what is meant by whoever interprets it as the even path and the straight path. Then what is intended by it is either the state of affairs in general or specifically the religion of Islam, and the first is more appropriate for the manifest excellence in relation to the two parts of the book. He said, 'made for us' — the adverb is either attached to 'made' and the lam is for benefit, as is said in His saying, 'And He has made for you the earth as a bed' (2:22), or it is attached to 'fellow' and there is the advancement of the object of the genitive construction...
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage explains two phrases from the original text. First, 'sawa' al-tariq' (the straight path) is defined as the middle of the road (wasatuhu) that surely leads the traveler to the goal. This is a metaphor (kinaya) for the even path (al-tariq al-mustawi) and the straight path (al-sirat al-mustaqim), which are mutually entailing. The commentator then notes that what is intended by this path is either the state of affairs in general (nafs al-amr 'umuman) or specifically the religion of Islam, and he prefers the first interpretation because it yields manifest excellence in relation to both parts of the book (presumably the commentary and the original). Second, the phrase 'ja'ala lana' (made for us) is analyzed syntactically: the adverb 'lana' can be attached to the verb 'ja'ala' with the lam indicating benefit (li al-intifa'), as in the Qur'anic verse 'wa ja'ala lakumu al-arda firashan' (2:22). Alternatively, it could be attached to a companion (rafiq) and then there is a fronting of the object of the genitive construction. The text cuts off here, but the grammatical point is to show the possible readings of the phrase.

Key Terms
سواء الطريق (sawa' al-tariq): The middle of the road that inevitably leads the traveler to the desired goal; a metaphor for the straight path.
اللام (al-lam): The preposition meaning 'for', used here for benefit (li al-intifa') in the phrase 'ja'ala lana'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'sawa' al-tariq' metaphorically refer to?
A) The physical middle of a road.
B) The even path and the straight path, which are mutually entailing.
C) The religion of Islam exclusively.
D) Both the general state of affairs and the religion of Islam, with the first being more appropriate.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's analysis shows that precise definition and grammatical parsing are essential for understanding the intended meaning of key terms in logical discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a Qur'anic verse such as 'wa ja'ala lakumu al-arda firashan' (2:22), analyze the phrase 'ja'ala lakumu' using the two possible syntactic attachments discussed in the lesson. Which interpretation makes more sense in context, and why?
?
Find a term in an Islamic logical text (e.g., 'hadd' or 'qiyas') that has both a literal and a technical meaning. Write two possible interpretations and argue which one the author likely intended, following the commentator's method.

Two Definitions of Hidaya and Their Difference
Author presents two definitions of hidaya and their difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of hidaya and identify which one entails reaching the goal.

Core Idea
The first definition of hidaya entails arrival; the second only shows the way.

In this passage, the author presents two definitions of 'hidaya' (guidance) that have been proposed. Understanding the difference between them is crucial because each leads to different theological implications. This lesson will walk you through the two definitions and the key distinction: whether guidance necessarily delivers you to the goal or merely points the way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first definition
Find the phrase 'al-dalala al-muwsila ila al-matlub'. This defines hidaya as 'the indicative communication that leads to the sought object' – i.e., it actually delivers.
'al-muwsila' means 'that leads/delivers'.
2
Identify the second definition
Find 'ira'at al-tariq al-muwsil ila al-matlub'. This defines hidaya as 'showing the way that leads to the sought object' – i.e., merely indicating the path.
3
Analyze the key difference
The author states that the first entails (yastalzim) reaching the goal, while the second does not. Showing the way does not guarantee arrival; one might still go astray.
This difference is the basis for the objections that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «الذي هدانا» الهداية (۲۲) قيل: هي (۲۳) الدلالة الموصلة اى الايصال (۲۴) الى المطلوب وقيل : هی (۲۵) ارائة الطريق (۲۶) الموصل الى المطلوب. والفرق بين هذين المعنيين ان الأول يستلزم الوصول الى المطلوب بخلاف الثاني فان الدلالة على ما يوصل الى المطلوب لا يلزم ان تكون موصلة الى ما يوصل فكيف توصل الى المطلوب ؟ والاول منقوض بقوله تعالى: «و اما نمود فهديناهم فاستحبوا العمى على الهدى» (۲۷) اذلايتصور الضلال بعد الوصول الى الحق (۲۸). والثانى منقوض بقوله تعالى : انك لا تهدى من احببت ولكن الله يهدى من يشاء» (۲۹) فان النبي (ص) كان شأنه وانة الطريق (٣٠)) والذى يفهم من كلام المصنف فى حاشية الكشاف (۳۱) هو ان الهداية لفظ مشترك بين هذين المعنيين و ح (۳۲) يظهر اندفاع كلا النقضين و يرتفع الخلاف من البين. و محصول كلام المصنف فى تنك الحاشية: ان الهداية لفظ يتعدى الى المفعول الثاني تارة بنفسه نحو: «أهدنا الصراط المستقيم » (۳۳) وتارة بـ «الى» نحو: «والله يهدى من يشاء الى صراط مستقيم وتارة باللام (۳۴) نحو: «ان هذا القرآن يهدى للتي هي اقوم) فمعناها على الاستعمال الأول هو الايصال وعلى الثانى (۳۵) اراثة الطريق.
His saying 'who guided us': guidance (22). It was said: it is the indicative communication that leads, i.e., the delivery (24) to the sought object. And it was said: it is the showing of the way (26) that leads to the sought object. The difference between these two meanings is that the first entails reaching the sought object, unlike the second, for indicating what leads to the sought object does not necessarily lead to what leads, so how could it lead to the sought object? The first is refuted by His saying, exalted: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance' (27), for misguidance cannot be imagined after reaching the truth (28). The second is refuted by His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills' (29), for the Prophet's (peace be upon him) role was showing the way (30). What is understood from the author's words in his gloss on al-Kashshaf (31) is that 'hidaya' is a homonym shared between these two meanings, and then (32) the repulsion of both refutations appears and the disagreement is removed. The gist of the author's words in that gloss is that 'hidaya' is a word that sometimes takes the second object by itself, as in 'Guide us to the straight path' (33), and sometimes with 'to', as in 'And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path', and sometimes with 'lam' (34), as in 'Indeed, this Qur'an guides to that which is most upright' (35). So its meaning on the first usage is delivery, and on the second (35) showing the way.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the meaning of 'hidaya' (guidance). The author presents two definitions: first, guidance as indicative communication that actually leads to the desired end (al-dalala al-muwsila), and second, as merely showing the way (ira'at al-tariq) that leads to the desired end. The key difference is that the first entails reaching the goal, while the second does not—showing the way does not guarantee arrival. The author then raises objections: for the first definition, the Qur'anic verse about Thamud (41:17) says Allah guided them, yet they went astray, which contradicts the idea that guidance entails reaching the truth. For the second definition, the verse about the Prophet (28:56) says he cannot guide whom he loves, whereas the second definition would allow him to show the way (which he did). The author resolves the conflict by citing the commentator (al-Zamakhshari?) who holds that 'hidaya' is a homonym (lafz mushtarak) having both meanings. By recognizing this, both objections are deflected. The author then summarizes the homonymy: the word takes its object directly (as in 'ihdina al-sirat al-mustaqim') meaning delivery, or with the preposition 'ila' or 'lam' meaning showing the way.

Key Terms
الهداية
الدلالة الموصلة
إراءة الطريق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between the two definitions of hidaya?
A) The first is a command, the second is a request.
B) The first entails reaching the goal, the second does not.
C) The first is from Allah, the second is from the Prophet.
D) The first is about belief, the second is about action.

Key Takeaway
The two definitions of hidaya differ in whether guidance necessarily delivers one to the goal or merely shows the path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the command 'Establish prayer'. Does the command itself entail that the prayer is accepted (first meaning) or just that the way is indicated (second meaning)? Use the two definitions of hidaya to analyze.

Commentary Notes
intermediate
5 min
Resolving Objections Through Homonymy
Author raises objections to definitions and resolves by homonymy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of homonymy (ishtirak) to resolve apparent contradictions between Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Recognizing 'hidaya' as homonymous resolves the conflict between verses that seemingly refute each definition.

The author now raises two objections, each targeting one of the definitions using Quranic evidence. After presenting the objections, he shows that the resolution lies in recognizing that 'hidaya' is a homonym (mushtarak) – a word with two distinct meanings. This lesson will guide you through the dialectical structure and the clever resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The verse about Thamud (41:17) says 'We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance.' If guidance means delivery, they would have reached truth and could not later go astray. So this refutes the first definition.
Objection targets the entailment of arrival.
2
Identify the second objection
The verse (28:56) says 'You do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills.' If guidance means showing the way, the Prophet did show the way, contradicting the verse. So this refutes the second definition.
Objection targets the scope of agency.
3
Understand the resolution via homonymy
The author cites the gloss on al-Kashshaf: 'hidaya' is a homonym (lafz mushtarak) shared between the two meanings. In the Thamud verse, it means delivery (first meaning) – they were delivered to truth but rejected. In the Prophet's case, it means showing the way (second meaning) – Allah alone delivers. Thus no contradiction.
Each verse uses a different sense of hidaya.
4
Apply the grammatical distinction
The author adds: when hidaya takes its object directly (e.g., 'ihdina'), it means delivery; when with the prepositions 'ila' or 'lam', it means showing the way. This grammatical pattern reinforces the homonymy.
This helps identify which meaning is intended in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «الذي هدانا» الهداية (۲۲) قيل: هي (۲۳) الدلالة الموصلة اى الايصال (۲۴) الى المطلوب وقيل : هی (۲۵) ارائة الطريق (۲۶) الموصل الى المطلوب. والفرق بين هذين المعنيين ان الأول يستلزم الوصول الى المطلوب بخلاف الثاني فان الدلالة على ما يوصل الى المطلوب لا يلزم ان تكون موصلة الى ما يوصل فكيف توصل الى المطلوب ؟ والاول منقوض بقوله تعالى: «و اما نمود فهديناهم فاستحبوا العمى على الهدى» (۲۷) اذلايتصور الضلال بعد الوصول الى الحق (۲۸). والثانى منقوض بقوله تعالى : انك لا تهدى من احببت ولكن الله يهدى من يشاء» (۲۹) فان النبي (ص) كان شأنه وانة الطريق (٣٠)) والذى يفهم من كلام المصنف فى حاشية الكشاف (۳۱) هو ان الهداية لفظ مشترك بين هذين المعنيين و ح (۳۲) يظهر اندفاع كلا النقضين و يرتفع الخلاف من البين. و محصول كلام المصنف فى تنك الحاشية: ان الهداية لفظ يتعدى الى المفعول الثاني تارة بنفسه نحو: «أهدنا الصراط المستقيم » (۳۳) وتارة بـ «الى» نحو: «والله يهدى من يشاء الى صراط مستقيم وتارة باللام (۳۴) نحو: «ان هذا القرآن يهدى للتي هي اقوم) فمعناها على الاستعمال الأول هو الايصال وعلى الثانى (۳۵) اراثة الطريق.
His saying 'who guided us': guidance (22). It was said: it is the indicative communication that leads, i.e., the delivery (24) to the sought object. And it was said: it is the showing of the way (26) that leads to the sought object. The difference between these two meanings is that the first entails reaching the sought object, unlike the second, for indicating what leads to the sought object does not necessarily lead to what leads, so how could it lead to the sought object? The first is refuted by His saying, exalted: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance' (27), for misguidance cannot be imagined after reaching the truth (28). The second is refuted by His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills' (29), for the Prophet's (peace be upon him) role was showing the way (30). What is understood from the author's words in his gloss on al-Kashshaf (31) is that 'hidaya' is a homonym shared between these two meanings, and then (32) the repulsion of both refutations appears and the disagreement is removed. The gist of the author's words in that gloss is that 'hidaya' is a word that sometimes takes the second object by itself, as in 'Guide us to the straight path' (33), and sometimes with 'to', as in 'And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path', and sometimes with 'lam' (34), as in 'Indeed, this Qur'an guides to that which is most upright' (35). So its meaning on the first usage is delivery, and on the second (35) showing the way.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the meaning of 'hidaya' (guidance). The author presents two definitions: first, guidance as indicative communication that actually leads to the desired end (al-dalala al-muwsila), and second, as merely showing the way (ira'at al-tariq) that leads to the desired end. The key difference is that the first entails reaching the goal, while the second does not—showing the way does not guarantee arrival. The author then raises objections: for the first definition, the Qur'anic verse about Thamud (41:17) says Allah guided them, yet they went astray, which contradicts the idea that guidance entails reaching the truth. For the second definition, the verse about the Prophet (28:56) says he cannot guide whom he loves, whereas the second definition would allow him to show the way (which he did). The author resolves the conflict by citing the commentator (al-Zamakhshari?) who holds that 'hidaya' is a homonym (lafz mushtarak) having both meanings. By recognizing this, both objections are deflected. The author then summarizes the homonymy: the word takes its object directly (as in 'ihdina al-sirat al-mustaqim') meaning delivery, or with the preposition 'ila' or 'lam' meaning showing the way.

Key Terms
مشترك
نقض
حاشية الكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the objection against the second definition of hidaya?
A) By rejecting the verse as weak.
B) By arguing that the Prophet does show the way, but the verse denies delivery.
C) By asserting that hidaya is a homonym and the verse uses the first meaning.
D) By redefining showing the way to include delivery.

Key Takeaway
Homonymy allows a single word to have multiple valid meanings, resolving apparent contradictions in scripture.

Defining 'Sawa' al-Tariq': Middle of the Road as a Metaphor for Truth
Author defines 'sawa' al-tariq' as middle of road, metaphor for straight path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of a key term from an Islamic text by identifying its literal meaning, figurative extension, and interpretive scope.

Core Idea
A single phrase can carry multiple layers of meaning; the commentator teases them out.

When we encounter an unfamiliar phrase in a classical Islamic text, we must first understand its literal sense, then its figurative usage, and finally the range of interpretations intended by the author. This lesson walks through that process using the commentator's explanation of 'sawa' al-tariq' from the opening of a logic textbook.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Literal Meaning
What does 'sawa' al-tariq' literally mean? The author explicitly says it means 'its middle' (wasat). Note that this is the basic lexical definition.
2
Recognize the Metaphorical Extension
The author calls this a metaphor (kinaya) for 'the straight road' and 'the straight path'. Understand that 'middle of the road' is a figure of speech for a path that is straight and leads surely to the goal.
The phrase 'necessarily leads its traveler to the sought object' reinforces the idea of certainty.
3
Determine the Two Interpretations
What does 'sawa' al-tariq' refer to in this context? The author presents two possibilities: (1) the absolute truth in general (nafs al-amr), or (2) specifically the religion of Islam (millat al-Islam).
4
Evaluate the Author's Preference
The author considers the first interpretation (general truth) as 'more appropriate' (awla) because it yields 'manifest excellence' in relation to the two parts of the book. Think about why a broader interpretation might be preferred for a foundational term.
The reasoning is based on the aim of the book, which likely addresses both believers and non-believers.

قوله « سواء الطريق»: اى وسطه (۳۶) الذي يفضي سالكه الى المطلوب البتة (۳۷) و هذا كناية (۳۸) عن الطريق المستوى (۳۹) والصراط المستقم اذهما متلازمان وهذا مراد من فسّره بالطريق المستوى و الصراط المستقيم. ثم المراد به (۴۰) اما نفس الأمر عموماً او خصوص ملة الاسلام (۴۱)، والاول اولى (۴۲) لخحصول البراعة الظاهرة بالقياس الى قسمي الكتاب.

This is a metaphor (38) for the straight road (39) and the straight path, for they are concomitant. This is what is meant by those who interpret it as the straight road and the straight path. Then what is intended by it (40) is either the matter itself generally or the specific religion of Islam (41), and the first is more appropriate (42) for obtaining manifest excellence in relation to the two parts of the book.

This is a metaphor (kinaya) for the straight road (al-tariq al-mustawi) and the straight path (al-sirat al-mustaqim), which are mutually entailing. The author then clarifies that what is intended by this phrase is either the absolute truth (nafs al-amr) in general or specifically the religion of Islam (millat al-Islam). He prefers the first interpretation because it demonstrates more obvious excellence with respect to the two parts of the book (likely the introduction and the main body).

Syntactic Attachment of 'Lana' in 'Ja'ala Lana'
Grammatical analysis of 'ja'ala lana' attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two possible syntactic attachments of a prepositional phrase in a classical Arabic text and determine which is preferred based on wording and meaning.

Core Idea
A single phrase can have multiple valid attachments; the best choice balances grammar with meaning.

In this passage, the author of the hashia examines the phrase 'ja'ala lana' from the book's opening. He presents two possible ways to attach the prepositional phrase 'lana': either to the verb or to an implied noun. This grammatical analysis is not mere pedantry; it reflects the precision required in Islamic scholarly writing, where every word's placement carries significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase under analysis
The phrase is 'ja'ala lana' (He made for us). The author discusses the attachment of the word 'lana' (for us).
2
Extract the first attachment option
Option one: 'lana' is attached to the verb 'ja'ala', with the 'lam' indicating benefit (lām al-intifāʿ). This is supported by the Qur'anic verse 'Wa ja'ala lakum al-arda firāshan' (And He made for you the earth as a bed).
3
Extract the second attachment option
Option two: 'lana' is attached to an implied noun 'rafīq' (companion). This is suggested by the later phrase 'al-tawfīqu khayru rafīq' (divine success is the best companion).
4
Note the syntactic reasoning
In option two, the word 'lana' is advanced before its genitive (the implied 'rafīq') because it is an adverb (ẓarf), and adverbs can be advanced in Arabic syntax.
5
Evaluate the two options
The author states that the first option is closer in wording (aqrabu lafẓan) because it directly attaches to the verb, while the second is closer in meaning (aqrabu maʿnan) because it aligns with the thematic praise of tawfīq as a companion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله جعل لنا» الظرف (۴۳) اما متعلق بجعل واللام للانتفاع كما قيل في قوله تعالى: (وجعل لكم الارض فراشاً» (۴۴) و اما برفيق و يكون تقديم معمول المضاف في خطبة الكتاب التوفيق (ه) خير رفيق والصلوة على من ارسله هدى (ه) اليه على المضاف لكونه ظرفاً والظرف ممّا يتوسع فيه (۴۵) و الاول اقرب لفظاً (۴۶) و الثاني معنى.
His saying 'ja'ala lana': the adverb (43) is either attached to 'ja'ala' and the 'lam' is for benefit, as was said in His saying, exalted: 'And He made for you the earth as a bed' (44), or (it is attached) to 'rafiq' (companion) and the advancing of the object of the genitive in the sermon of the book: 'al-tawfiq (h) is the best companion and prayers upon the one He sent as guidance (h) over the genitive because it is an adverb, and the adverb is something that is treated loosely (45). The first is closer in wording (46) and the second in meaning.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical commentary on the phrase 'ja'ala lana' (He made for us) from the book's opening. The author discusses the syntactic attachment of the prepositional phrase 'lana' (for us). There are two possibilities: (1) it is attached to the verb 'ja'ala' (made), and the 'lam' indicates benefit (lam al-intifa'), analogous to the Qur'anic verse 'Ja'ala lakum al-arda firashan' (He made for you the earth as a bed). (2) It is attached to an implied noun 'rafiq' (companion), based on the subsequent phrase 'al-tawfiq khayru rafiq' (divine success is the best companion). In this second option, the phrase 'lana' is placed before its genitive (the implied 'rafiq') because it is an adverb, and adverbs can be advanced. The author notes that the first option is closer in wording (since it directly attaches to the verb), while the second is closer in meaning (since it aligns with the later praise of tawfiq as a companion).

لام الانتفاع
رفيق

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'lana' can be attached either to the verb 'ja'ala' or to an implied noun 'rafiq', and the author prefers the first for wording but the second for meaning.

Defining Tawfiq: Genus, Differentia, and Commentary Expansion
Author defines tawfiq as directing causes toward the desired good, with marginal expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of tawfiq and explain how the marginal note expands the base text's definition.

Core Idea
Tawfiq is not success itself but God's directing of means toward the good.

This lesson unpacks a brief definition of tawfiq from a classical logic text. You will learn to dissect a definition into its components and see how a commentator refines it. This skill is essential for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences, where precision matters for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its base definition
Find the term being defined (tawfiq) and the base text's statement: 'it is directing the means towards the sought good.' Note the key elements: 'directing' (tawjih), 'means' (asbab), 'sought good' (al-matlub al-khayr).
2
Extract genus and differentia
Treat the definition as a logical definition. The genus is 'directing' (tawjih) or 'making agree' (muwafaqa) – the broader action. The differentia is 'towards the sought good' – what distinguishes tawfiq from other acts of directing.
This is a rasm taam (complete description) rather than a hadd haqiqi, as it describes the function.
3
Compare with the marginal note expansion
Read the marginal note: 'al-tawfiq is making the means agree with the sought object, and its essence is directing the means towards the effects.' Notice it replaces 'directing' with 'making agree' and adds 'effects' (al-musabbabat) as the target.
The expansion shifts focus from 'directing' to 'agreement' and specifies 'effects' as the outcome.
4
Understand what the expansion adds
The marginal note clarifies that tawfiq involves a harmony (muwafaqa) between means and end, not just any direction. It also defines the essence as directing means toward the results (musabbabat), emphasizing the causal link.
This refinement prevents confusion: tawfiq is not arbitrary direction but precise alignment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « التوفيق»: هو توجيه الاسباب نحو المطلوب الخير (٤٧) (ه) قوله وجعل لنا التوفيق: التوفيق جعل الاسباب موافقة للمطلوب و حاصله توجیه الاسباب نحو المسببات.
His saying 'al-tawfiq': it is directing the means towards the sought good (47). (h) His saying 'and He made for us al-tawfiq': al-tawfiq is making the means agree with the sought object, and its essence is directing the means towards the effects.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-tawfiq' (divine success). In the main text, the author states that tawfiq is the directing (tawjih) of means (asbab) towards the desired good (al-matlub al-khayr). The marginal note expands this: tawfiq is making the means agree or correspond (muwafaqa) with the sought object, and its essence is directing the means towards the outcomes (al-musabbabat). This definition emphasizes that tawfiq is an act of God that aligns the means (like effort, circumstances) to achieve a good end. It is not the success itself but the divine facilitation of the means.

Key Terms
التوفيق
الاسباب
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what is the essence (hasil) of tawfiq?
A) The achievement of the goal.
B) Directing the means towards the outcomes.
C) Good fortune.
D) The means themselves.

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq is a relational concept: God's directing means to harmonize with the desired good, not the good itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Salat and the Honorific Description of the Prophet
Author defines salat as supplication and explains why the Prophet is referred to indirectly as 'the one sent'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the lexical and theological definition of salat and identify the rationale behind the Prophet's indirect reference as 'the one He sent'.

Core Idea
Salat from God is mercy, not a request; and 'the one sent' is the perfect description of the Prophet.

This passage from a classic commentary unpacks two key elements of a common phrase. First, the word 'prayer' (salat) is defined linguistically and theologically. Second, the Prophet is referred to not by name but by his mission—'the one He sent.' Understanding these nuances sharpens your ability to analyze theological language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two explained elements
The commentator explains two parts of a phrase: 'al-salatu' (prayer) and 'ala man arsalahu' (upon the one He sent). Recognize that these are separate but connected explanations.
2
Analyze the definition of salat
Note that 'salat' literally means supplication (du'a), which is a request for mercy. However, when attributed to Allah, the meaning shifts metaphorically to mercy itself, because requesting mercy from God is inappropriate for His majesty.
This is a common pattern: words like 'shafa'a' (intercession) also shift meaning when applied to God.
3
Understand the indirect reference to the Prophet
The Prophet is not named directly; instead he is described as 'the one He sent' (man arsalahu). The commentator gives three reasons: glorification, uniqueness (the description immediately identifies him), and that this attribute implies all perfections and explicitly states his messengership.
4
Distinguish between risala and nubuwwa
The passage asserts that messengership (risala) is higher than prophethood (nubuwwa) because a messenger receives a religion and a book. This theological ranking is significant in Islamic thought.
This ranking is not universally agreed upon; some scholars equate them or rank prophethood higher, but here the commentator argues for the superiority of risala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « والصلوة»: هي بمعنى الدعاء (۴۸) اى طلب الرحمة (۴۹) و اذا اسند الى الله (۵۰) تجرد عن معنى الطلب و يراد به الرحمة مجازاً. قوله «على من ارسله»: لم يصرح باسمه تعظيماً (۵۱) و اجلالاً و تنبيهاً على انه (ص) فيما ذكر من الوصف بمرتبة لا يتبادر الذهن منه الا اليه واختار (۵۲) من بين الصفات هذه لكونها مستلزمة لسائر (۵۳) الصفات الكمالية مع ما فيه من التصريح بكونه مرسلا، فان (۵۴) مرتبة الرسالة فوق النبوة (۵۵) فان المرسل الذي ارسل اليه دين و کتاب.
His saying 'and prayer': it means supplication (48), i.e., requesting mercy (49). And when it is attributed to Allah (50), it becomes stripped of the meaning of request and is intended metaphorically as mercy. His saying 'upon the one He sent': he did not mention his name explicitly as a glorification (51) and exaltation and to indicate that he (peace be upon him), in what was mentioned of the description, is in a rank that the mind does not go from it except to him. And he chose (52) from among the attributes this one because it entails all the other attributes of perfection (53), along with what it contains of explicitly stating that he is a messenger. For (54) the rank of messengership is above prophethood (55), for the messenger is one to whom a religion and a book are sent.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage explains two parts of the opening phrase: 'al-salatu' and 'ala man arsalahu'. First, 'salat' (prayer) is defined as supplication (du'a), i.e., requesting mercy. When attributed to God (as in 'salat Allah'), it loses the meaning of request and is used metaphorically to mean mercy itself. Second, the phrase 'ala man arsalahu' (upon the one He sent) refers to the Prophet Muhammad without naming him explicitly. This is for glorification and exaltation, and to point out that the description (the one sent) is so unique that the mind immediately goes to him. The author notes that the attribute 'the one sent' (al-mursal) was chosen because it entails all other perfect attributes, as it explicitly states he is a messenger. The passage then asserts that the rank of messengership (risala) is higher than prophethood (nubuwwa), because the messenger is given a religion and a book.

Key Terms
الصلوة
الدعاء
الرسالة
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is shorter.
B) Because it implies all perfect attributes and explicitly states he is a messenger.
C) Because the Prophet disliked his name.
D) Because it was a common expression.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic terms shift meaning when applied to Allah (e.g., salat becomes mercy), and describing the Prophet by his mission highlights his unique rank and perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Two Definitions of Fikr (Thought) by Early and Later Scholars
Marginal note contrasts early and later definitions of fikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the early and later definitions of fikr and explain how the matn's definition accommodates both.

Core Idea
The definition of thought shifted from a double movement to pure ordering — a refinement that makes logical structure the essence.

This marginal note from a logic commentary addresses a key epistemological shift in Islamic logic. The earlier scholars defined thought (fikr) as a double movement of the mind from the sought conclusion to the premises and then back. The later scholars refined this to focus solely on the ordering of premises to reach the unknown. The commentator shows that the matn's concise definition, 'contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown,' elegantly covers both interpretations, resolving potential conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
In the Arabic passage, find where the early scholars (المتقدمون) define fikr as a double movement, and where the later scholars (المتأخرون) define it as ordering. Note the key terms: حركة and ترتيب.
2
Understand the reason for the shift
The commentator explains that the first movement (from the unknown to premises) can occur without thought (e.g., by chance). Only the second movement (ordering) is essential. Why does this matter for logical precision?
3
See how the matn's definition covers both
The matn says: 'ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول'. The commentator argues this applies to both schools. Explain how the phrase 'ملاحظة المعقول' can include both the search for premises and the ordering, and 'لتحصيل المجهول' emphasizes the goal.
This shows the commentator's skill in reconciling schools.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objection is raised: the later definition of ordering doesn't apply to definitions using simple terms like 'rational' (الناطق) because there is no apparent ordering. The response: such terms are conceptually composite or require mental transfer. How does this defense preserve the later definition?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) قوله والاكتساب بالنظر: انما قيد الاكتساب بالنظر مع ان الاكتساب في العرف بل في اللغة يطلق حيث يكون في متعلقه تعب وسعى اذ قد يحصل الاكتساب بالصدفة والاتفاق. (ه) قول المصنف «و هو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول اعلم ان التعريف المشهور للنظر و الفكر عند المتقدمين هو ان الفكر حركة ذهن الانسان من المطالب نحو المبادى و الرجوع عنها الى المطالب وعند المتأخرين هو ترتيب امور معلومة للتأدى الى المجهول. بيان ذلك انه اذا اريد تحصیل مجهول مشعور به من وجه انتقل الذهن منه و تحركت نحو المعقولات الى ان تجد مبادى هذا المطلوب و يتصورها ثم ينتقل منها بترتيبها الى ذلك المجهول المطلوب فيحصل هناك انتقالان، فالمتقدمون على ان الفعل المتوسط بين المعلومات و المجهولات الذي هو عبارة عن النظر هو مجموع الانتقالين اذ به يتوصل من المعلوم نحو المجهول والمتأخرون على انه عبارة عن الترتيب الحاصل من الانتقال الثاني اذ تحصيل المجهول من المعلوم يدور عليه وجوداً وعدماً والانتقالان خارجان عن الفكر الا ان الثاني منهما لازم له اذ لا يوجد بدونه البتة دون الاول لحصوله بدونه في بعض الاوقات فاذا انطبع هذا على صحيفة الخاطر، فنقول عرف المصنف النظر بملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول ينطبق على كلا المذهبين عليه ما اورد على تعريف المتأخرين من انه لايتناول التعريف بالفصل وحده ولا بالخاصة وجدها لظهور انه لا ترتيب حينئذ انه يصح التعريف بهما عندهم كما سيصرح به المصنف في فصل المعرف حتى يحتاج الى الجواب بان التعريف بالمفردات انما يكون بالمشتقات كالناطق والضاحك والمشتق و ان كان فى اللفظ مفرداً الا ان معناه شيء له المشتق منه فيكون من حيث المعنى مركبا، أو بان الفصل والخاصة لا يدلان على المطلوب الابقرينة عقلية موجبة لانتقال الذهن اليه فالتركيب لازم لا محالة.
(5) His statement 'and acquisition is by reflection': He only specified acquisition by reflection, even though acquisition in custom and language is used where there is effort and striving, because acquisition can occur by chance and coincidence. (Marginal note) The author's statement 'it is the contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown': Know that the well-known definition of reflection and thought among the early scholars (mutaqaddimun) is that thought is the movement of the human mind from the sought conclusions towards the premises and the return from them to the conclusions. And among the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun), it is the ordering of known matters to arrive at the unknown. Explanation: When one wants to acquire an unknown that is consciously perceived from one aspect, the mind transfers from it and moves towards intelligibles until it finds the premises of this sought conclusion and conceives them, then it transfers from them by ordering them to that unknown sought conclusion. So there are two transfers. The early scholars hold that the intermediate act between knowns and unknowns, which is called reflection, is the sum of the two transfers, because by it one proceeds from the known to the unknown. The later scholars hold that it is the ordering resulting from the second transfer, because the acquisition of the unknown from the known depends on it both in existence and non-existence, and the two transfers are external to thought, though the second is necessary for it (thought cannot exist without it) but not the first, as the first sometimes occurs without thought. So when this is engraved on the tablet of the mind, we say: The author defined reflection as 'contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown', and it applies to both schools. Regarding the objection against the definition of the later scholars that it does not cover definition by differentia alone or by property alone, because clearly there is no ordering in that case, the definition by them is valid, as the author will state explicitly in the chapter on definition, so that it is necessary to answer that definition by simple terms is only through derivatives like 'rational' and 'laughing', and the derivative, though simple in wording, its meaning is 'something that has the derivative from', so it is composite in meaning, or that the differentia and property do not indicate the required except by a mental reasoning that necessitates mental transfer, so composition is inevitable.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This marginal note provides a detailed epistemological discussion on the definition of fikr (thought) and nazar (reflection). It begins by commenting on the matn's phrase 'acquisition is by reflection', noting that normally acquisition implies effort, but here it is qualified by 'by reflection' because acquisition can occur by chance. Then it presents the famous two definitions: the early scholars (mutaqaddimun) define fikr as the double movement of the mind: from the sought conclusion (matlub) to the premises (mabadi), and then back from the premises to the conclusion. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) define it as merely the ordering (tartib) of known premises to arrive at the unknown, considering the two movements as external to the act of thought, although the second movement is necessary. The explanation is that when seeking an unknown, the mind first moves from the felt unknown to the known intelligibles (the first transfer), then once the premises are found, it orders them and moves back to the unknown (the second transfer). The early scholars consider the whole process as thought; the later scholars consider only the ordering (second transfer) as thought, because the acquisition depends on it, and the first transfer can occur without thought (e.g., by accident). The author's definition ('contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown') is shown to be compatible with both schools. Then the note addresses an objection to the later scholars' definition: definitions using simple terms like differentia (fasl) or property (khassa) do not involve ordering, contradicting the definition. The answer is that such simple terms are actually derivative (e.g., 'rational' implies 'something that has reason'), so they are composite in meaning and require ordering in the mind. Alternatively, they require a mental reasoning that transfers the mind, so composition (tarkib) is inevitable. This shows the sophistication of the later definition.

Key Terms
الفكر (al-fikr): Thought: either the double movement of the mind from conclusion to premises and back (mutaqaddimun) or the ordering of known premises to arrive at the unknown (muta'akhkhirun). [Note: The definition shifted from a process of movement to an ordering; the later definition is more precise and focuses on the logical structure.]
المتقدمون (al-mutaqaddimun): Early scholars: those who defined nazar/fikr as the total double movement. [Note: In other contexts, mutaqaddimun can refer to early philosophers or theologians; here specifically logicians.]
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): Later scholars: those who defined nazar/fikr as the ordering alone. [Note: This distinction is common in later logic (post-Razi).]
الترتيب (al-tartib): Ordering: the arrangement of premises to produce the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun), what is the essential act of thought (fikr)?
A) The movement from the conclusion to the premises.
B) The ordering of the premises to reach the conclusion.
C) Both the first and second movements combined.
D) The sudden acquisition of knowledge without movement.

Key Takeaway
The later scholars refined the definition of thought to focus solely on the ordering of premises, making logical structure the essence of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Compare the early and later definitions of fikr in your own words. Which definition do you think better captures the essence of rational thought in Islamic theology? Justify your answer.
?
2. Read the objection in the passage about definitions using simple terms. Summarize the commentator's response. Then, using your own example from Islamic logic (e.g., defining 'human' as 'rational animal'), explain how ordering is involved even in such simple definitions.

The Nature of Ma'rif as Qawl Sharh
Commentator explains 'ma'rif' as qawl sharh and its composition conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'ma'rif' is called 'qawl sharh' and assess the conditions under which an incomplete definition qualifies as a 'qawl'.

Core Idea
A definition is called a 'qawl sharh' because it explains essences, and 'qawl' implies composition—even when the wording is singular.

This lesson unpacks the term 'ma'rif' as used in logic. The commentator clarifies its synonym 'qawl sharh' and discusses the issue of composition in definitions. You will learn how logicians classify definitions and why even a single-word differentia may be considered compound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of qawl sharh
Note that 'sharh' explains essence, 'qawl' indicates a statement, usually compound. Find this in the Arabic: 'اما شارحا فلشرحه ماهية الشيء وحقيقته و اما قولا فلانه فى الاغلب مركب'.
2
Trace the disagreement on compoundness
Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif argues that complete definitions are always compound; incomplete definitions may be singular or compound depending on whether one allows definition by differentia alone.
The text says: 'الحد التام مركب قطعاً والحد الناقص قد يكون مركباً وقد لا يكون'.
3
Resolve the issue using prior knowledge
The commentator recalls from an earlier discussion that even a singular term like the differentia alone is actually compound in logical reality ('في الحقيقة مركبان'). Therefore the restriction to 'mostly' is unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the exception
Consider the claim that 'qawl' only applies to overtly compound expressions. The commentator invites reflection: 'الا ان يدعى ان القول لا يطلق الا على المركب الذي يكون تركيبه ظاهراً فتأمل'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «معرفاً»: لانه يعرف و يبين حال المجهول التصوري (۸۱) قوله «حجة»: لانها تصير سبباً للغلبة على الخصم والحجة في اللغة الغلبة، فهذا من قبيل تسمية السبب باسم المسبب (۸۲) (ه) قوله فيسمى معرفاً وقد يسمى قولاً شارحاً ايضاً اما «شارحاً» فلشرحه ماهية الشيء وحقيقته و اما «قولاً» فلانه فى الاغلب مركب و القول يرادفه كذا قال بعض المحققين في شرح الرسالة و قال المحقق الشريف: وذلك لان الحد التام مركب قطعاً والحد الناقص قد يكون مركباً وقد لا يكون عند من من جوز الحد الناقص بالفصل وحده والرسم التام مركب قطعاً والرسم الناقص قد يكون مركباً وقد لا يكون عند من جوز الرسم الناقص بالخاصة وحدها انتهى. و قد عرفت فيما سبق فى تعريف النظر ان الحد الناقص والرسم الناقص اذا كانا بالفصل وحده والخاصة وحدها فهما و ان كانا بحسب اللفظ مفردين لكنهما في الحقيقة مركبان فلاحاجة ح الى التقييد بالاغلبية. اللهم الا ان يدعى ان القول لا يطلق الا على المركب الذي يكون تركيبه ظاهراً فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) (ه) قوله فيسمى حجة الحجة في اللغة الغلبة ، يقال : حج يحج اذا غلب، ولفظ اللغة مأخوذ من لغى يلغى اذا لهج بالكلام وفى الصحاح ان اصلها لغي او لغو و الهاء عوض وجمعها لغي مثل «برة» و «برى و لغات ايضاً وقال بعضهم: سمعت لغاتهم بفتح التاء وشبهها بالتاء التي يوقف عليها بالهاء والنسبة اليها لغوي (عبد الرحيم)
His saying 'ma'rif': because it makes known and clarifies the state of the conceptual unknown. His saying 'hujja': because it becomes a cause for overcoming the opponent; and al-hujja in language means overcoming. So this is of the type of naming the cause by the name of the effect. (H) His saying 'it is called ma'rif, and it is also called qawl sharh. As for 'sharh', because it explains the quiddity and reality of the thing; and as for 'qawl', because it is mostly compound, and qawl is synonymous with it. Such said some investigators in the commentary on the Risala. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: 'That is because the complete definition is necessarily compound, and the incomplete definition may be compound and may not be compound according to whoever allows incomplete definition by the differentia alone. The complete description is necessarily compound, and the incomplete description may be compound and may not be compound according to whoever allows incomplete description by the property alone.' End. And you have known in what preceded in the definition of al-nazar that the incomplete definition and incomplete description, if they are by the differentia alone and the property alone, then even if they are lexically singular, they are in reality compound. So there is no need to restrict to the majority. Unless it is claimed that 'qawl' is only applied to a compound whose composition is apparent. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (H) His saying 'it is called hujja': al-hujja in language is overcoming. It is said: hajj yahujju when he overcomes. And the wording lugha is derived from laghi yalgha when he persists in speech. And in al-Sihah, its origin is laghi or laghw, and the ha' is a substitute, and its plural is lughi like 'burra' and 'buri', and also lughat. And some said: I heard lughatuhum with fatha on the ta', and it resembles the ta' that is pausal with ha', and the relative is lughawi (Abd al-Rahim).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage contains the hashiya's comments on the terms 'ma'rif' and 'hujja'. The commentator first explains why the ma'rif is also called a 'qawl sharh' (explanatory statement). The 'sharh' part is because it explains the essence; the 'qawl' part because it is usually compound. He cites al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif who notes that a complete definition is always compound, while incomplete definitions may be singular or compound depending on whether one allows a definition by differentia alone. The commentator adds from earlier discussion that even singular terms like differentia or property alone are actually compound in meaning, so the restriction to 'mostly' is unnecessary. He then leaves room for the claim that 'qawl' only applies to overtly compound expressions. The second footnote discusses the linguistic meaning of 'hujja'. It derives from the root h-j-j meaning to overcome, and the term is from the realm of 'lugha' (language) which itself derives from 'laghi' meaning to persist. The commentator provides morphological notes: the ha' is a substitute, plural forms, and a variant pronunciation.

معرفاً

B) The incomplete definition

Key Takeaway
The term 'ma'rif' is equivalent to 'qawl sharh' because it explains essences, and the qualification of composition is nuanced: even a single-word differentia is compound in logical reality.

The Linguistic Root of Hujja and Its Logical Application
Commentator explains linguistic origin of 'hujja' and its logical application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the linguistic derivation of 'hujja' from the root meaning 'overcoming' and explain the logical principle of naming the cause by the effect.

Core Idea
'Hujja' means 'overcoming' in language; its logical usage as a proof is a case of naming the cause (the proof) by its effect (overcoming the opponent).

This lesson explores the term 'hujja' (proof/argument) from its Arabic linguistic origins. The commentator shows that 'hujja' literally means 'overcoming' and that its logical sense is an instance of 'tasmiat al-sabab bi-ism al-musabbab' (naming the cause by the effect). Understanding this helps clarify why a valid argument is called a 'hujja'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root meaning
Find in the Arabic: 'الحجة في اللغة الغلبة' – al-hujja in language means overcoming. The root h-j-j conveys this.
2
Analyse the semantic transfer
The logical term hujja (proof) is the cause that leads to overcoming the opponent. Naming it 'hujja' is using the effect (overcoming) for the cause (the proof). This is called 'tasmiat al-sabab bi-ism al-musabbab'.
The text says: 'فهذا من قبيل تسمية السبب باسم المسبب'.
3
Examine the morphological notes
Note the derivation from 'hajja yahujju' (he overcame). Also note the alternative derivation from 'laghi' (to persist), with the ha' as a substitute. Plural forms include 'lughi' and 'lughat'.
The commentator provides linguistic details from al-Sihah.
4
Recognise the example in Islamic argumentation
In a formal debate (munazara), when a mutakallim provides a valid proof, that proof is his hujja because it enables him to overcome his opponent. The term reflects the purpose of argumentation.

Refining the Definition of Separable Accident
Author refines the definition of separable accident and clarifies the distinction between speed and ease of cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key refinement in the definition of separable accident and distinguish between speed of cessation and ease of removal.

Core Idea
Separable accident does not mean it always separates; it can persist, and speed does not imply ease.

In logic and metaphysics, precise definitions are crucial. The term 'separable accident' ('arad mufariq) can be misunderstood to imply that the accident must actually separate from its subject, or that it must do so quickly or slowly, with ease or difficulty. The author refines this definition to show that 'does not prevent separation' only indicates possibility, not actual occurrence, and that the speed of cessation is independent of the ease of removal. This lesson unpacks that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition under critique
The text implies an earlier definition that may have suggested a separable accident always separates or is restricted to quick or slow cessation. Pinpoint what the author is correcting: the assumption that 'does not prevent separation' entails actual separation.
The original definition is not explicitly quoted, but the correction shows it was too narrow.
2
Extract the author's correction
The author states: 'it does not follow that it is separated; it may persist.' The key is that possibility of separation does not mean separation happens. Also, the relationship (causal or coincidental) does not affect the definition.
The phrase 'infikāk' (separation) is more general than actual separation.
3
Analyze the speed vs. ease distinction
The text gives examples: quick cessation may be easy (bashfulness) or hard (love); slow cessation may be easy (youth) or hard (lameness). This shows that speed and ease are orthogonal. The commentator notes the examples are only for easy cases for clarity.
Reflect on why the commentator chose easy examples: to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و فيه ان العرض المفارق ما لا يمتنع انفكاكه عن المعروض ولا يلزم من ذلك ان يكون منفكا ينحصر فيهما بل يجوز ان لا يمتنع انفكاكه و يدوم فان الانفكاك اعم من ان يكون العلاقة مثل السببية و العلية أو بمجرد الاتفاق. قوله بسرعة او بطوء: اعلم ان سريع الزوال قديكون سهل الزوال كحمرة الخجل و قد يكون عسيره كالعشق و كذا البطىء قد يسهل زواله كالشباب و قد يعسر كالزمانة فالمحشى اكتفى في التمثيل بسهل الزوال لوضوحه و ظهوره
And in it: the separable accident is what does not prevent separation from its subject, but from that it does not follow that it is separated, limited to them two. Rather, it is permissible that its separation is not impossible and it persists. For separation is more general, whether the relation is like causality and causation or merely by coincidence. His statement 'quickly or slowly': know that the quick to disappear may be easy to disappear like the redness of bashfulness, and may be difficult like love. Similarly, the slow may have easy disappearance like youth, and may be difficult like lameness. So the commentator sufficed with examples of easy disappearance due to their clarity and prominence.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage critiques a previous definition of separable accident ('arad mufariq) that may have been understood to always separate. The author clarifies that 'does not prevent separation' does not mean it is actually separated; it may persist. The relationship can be causal or coincidental. Then it discusses the speed of cessation, noting that quick or slow does not imply ease or difficulty of removal, giving examples from natural qualities (bashfulness, love, youth, lameness). The commentator notes that the author's examples are only for easy cases because they are clearer.

Key Terms
عرض مفارق ('arad mufariq) – separable accident
انفكاك (infikāk) – separation
سريع الزوال (sarīʿ al-zawāl) – quick to disappear
بطيء الزوال (baṭīʾ al-zawāl) – slow to disappear

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is true about a separable accident?
A) It must separate from its subject at some point.
B) Its separation is always impossible.
C) It does not necessarily become separated; it may persist.
D) It only has easy cessation.

Key Takeaway
A separable accident is defined by the mere possibility of separation, not actual separation, and the speed of cessation is independent of ease of removal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the accident of 'whiteness' in a cloth. Explain how it fits the refined definition of separable accident: it can be removed (by dyeing), but it may persist indefinitely. Also, distinguish between the speed of removal (e.g., quick if bleached, slow if fading) and the ease of removal (e.g., easy with bleach, difficult with stubborn stains).
?
In a Kalam debate about the attribute of 'speech' (kalam) in God, someone argues that 'speech is a separable accident because it can be separated from the speaker'. Use the refinement to critique this claim.

Defending a Definition: Dialectical Responses to a Three-Part Disjunction
Commentator addresses an objection that disjunctions can have three parts, defending the standard definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the commentator's dialectical strategies for defending a definition against a counterexample.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can be met with multiple logical responses, each building on the previous.

When a definition is challenged by a counterexample, the defender must respond with precision. In this passage, the commentator defends the standard definition of disjunction (that it involves two opposite relations) against the objection that a word's division into noun, verb, and particle is a three-part disjunction. He offers six different responses, each presupposing the previous one has been conceded. This lesson unpacks those strategies and shows how they can be applied in Islamic intellectual debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector says: 'The disjunction may include three parts, as in their saying: A word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle.' This challenges the definition that a disjunction is about opposition of two relations.
The objector uses a familiar grammatical classification to raise the issue.
2
First Response: Reclassify the Statement
The commentator first denies that the statement is a disjunction; rather it is a categorical proposition with a divided predicate (ḥamliyya muraddada al-maḥmūl). This reclassifies the counterexample outside the scope of the definition.
This is a strong move: if the statement isn't a disjunction, it doesn't threaten the definition.
3
Second Response: Restrict the Domain
Even if conceded, the definition applies only to disjunctions in the philosophical sciences (al-‘ulūm al-ḥikmiyya), not to grammatical classifications. This limits the definition's scope to a specific field.
4
Third Response: Decompose into Binary Disjunctions
The three-part division can be understood as two binary disjunctions: first noun vs. non-noun, then non-noun divided into verb and particle. So it is really two two-part disjunctions, not one three-part.
This preserves the two-relation requirement by breaking down the complex division.
5
Fourth Response: Appeal to the Common Case
The definition is based on the common and most frequent case (al-a‘amm al-agh lab). Most disjunctions have two parts; exceptions do not invalidate the rule.
This is a standard strategy in definition-making.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن المنفصلة قد تشتمل على ثلاثة أجزاء كقولهم: الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل أو حرف، فكيف يصح القول بأن المنفصلة ما حكم فيها بتنافى النسبتين؟ قلنا: لا نسلم أن هذا القول منفصلة بل حملية مرددة المحمول، ولو سلم فنقول: إن التعريف المذكور للمنفصلة المعتبرة في العلوم الحكمية، وهذا القول إنما اعتبره الأدباء لا الحكماء، ولو سلم فنقول: إن هذا في الحقيقة منفصلتان مشتملتان على جزئين لا واحدة مشتملة على ثلاثة أجزاء، فكأنه قيل: الكلمة إما اسم أو غيره، وهو إما فعل أو حرف، ولو سلم فنقول: إن ذلك بناء على الأعم الأغلب، ولا شك أن الأغلب اشتمالها على جزئين، ولو سلم فهو بيان أقل المراتب، وما لا بد في وجودها وحصولها منه، ولو سلم فنقول: إن التثنية هنا إنما هو مجرد التعدد والتكرر لا الاثنينية، كما صرحوا بذلك في قولهم: «لبيك وسعديك».
If it is said: The disjunction may include three parts, as in their saying: 'A word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle.' So how is it correct to say that the disjunction is that in which judgment is made of the opposition of the two relations? We say: We do not concede that this statement is a disjunction; rather it is a categorical proposition with a divided predicate. And if it is conceded, we say: That definition refers to the disjunction considered in the philosophical sciences, and this statement is only considered by grammarians, not philosophers. And if it is conceded, we say: This is in reality two disjunctions each comprising two parts, not one comprising three parts; it is as if it were said: 'A word is either a noun or other than a noun, and that other is either a verb or a particle.' And if it is conceded, we say: That is based on the common and most frequent case, and there is no doubt that most disjunctions comprise two parts. And if it is conceded, then it is an explanation of the minimum, and that without which its existence and occurrence are not possible. And if it is conceded, we say: The duality here is merely multiplicity and repetition, not literal twoness, as they have clarified in their saying: 'Labbayka wa Sa‘dayka.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and response) typical in Islamic commentary literature. The objector points to a counterexample: the division of a word (kalima) into noun, verb, and particle has three parts, which seems to contradict the definition that a disjunction involves only two relations. The commentator provides multiple responses, each building on the previous if the objector does not concede: (1) The statement is not truly a disjunction but a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate (meaning the predicate is one of three things). (2) The standard definition applies to disjunctions used in the philosophical sciences (al-‘ulūm al-ḥikmiyya), not the grammatical classification used by grammarian. (3) The three-part division is actually two binary disjunctions: first between noun and non-noun, and then between verb and particle. (4) The definition is based on the common case (al-a‘amm al-agh lab), as most disjunctions have two parts. (5) The two-part requirement is the minimum; existence of a disjunction does not require exactly two. (6) The word 'two' (tathniya) here means multiplicity, not the number two, as in the Arabic expression 'Labbayka wa Sa‘dayka' which implies repetition, not twoness. This shows the thoroughness of the commentator in defending the core definition.

Key Terms
حملية مرددة المحمول
العلوم الحكمية
الأعم الأغلب
التثنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one of the commentator's responses to the objection that the division 'word is noun, verb, or particle' is a three-part disjunction?
A) He says it is an accidental disjunction.
B) He says it is actually a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate.
C) He says it is a compound syllogism.
D) He says it is an impossible statement.

Key Takeaway
A definition can be defended against counterexamples through multiple logical strategies, including reclassification, domain restriction, decomposition, appeal to commonality, minimality, and linguistic interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Suppose a logician defines 'proposition' as 'a statement that is either true or false.' An objector says: 'But what about a question? It is a statement, yet not true or false.' Defend the definition using at least two of the commentator's strategies from this lesson. Write your response as a short dialectical exchange.
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, the definition of qiyas (analogical reasoning) is often challenged by cases where the ‘illa is not explicitly stated. Take the definition 'Qiyas is extending a ruling from the original to the new case based on a shared effective cause.' How would you respond to an objector who says: 'But what about istihsan (juristic preference) which sometimes overrides qiyas?' Apply the 'common case' strategy.

Two Derivations of 'Basmala': Mimetic Word vs. Verbal Noun
Commentator discusses whether 'basmala' should be pronounced with kasra or fatha and its derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a mimetic word (muhqaqq) and a derived verbal noun (masdar ja'li) in Arabic linguistic coinages and identify the spelling implications for 'basmala'.

Core Idea
The spelling of 'basmala' reveals its linguistic nature — ta' indicates a verbal noun, ha' indicates a mimetic word.

The word 'basmala' is a shorthand for the phrase 'bismi Allah'. In Arabic scholarship, its pronunciation and spelling depend on whether it is treated as a direct mimetic utterance or as a coined verbal noun. This lesson unpacks the two views and the spelling problem they create.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two readings
The commentator presents two possible readings: with kasra on ba' and meem (mimetic) or with fatha on both (verbal noun). Find these in the Arabic text.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
On the first view, 'basmala' is a muhqaqq—a direct imitation of the phrase 'bismi Allah'. On the second, it is a masdar ja'li—a coined verbal noun like 'hawqala' from 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa bi-llah'.
The examples help clarify the distinction.
3
Recognize the spelling issue
On the first view, the word should end with ha (هاء) because it is mimetic, but common usage writes it with ta (تاء). On the second view, ta' is appropriate. The commentator notes this inconsistency.
This shows how linguistic analysis can affect orthography.
4
Apply to other Islamic coinages
Consider other similar coinages like 'hawqala', 'haylala', 'hasbala'. What does their spelling tell us about their derivation?
This reinforces the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسملة اما ان يقرء بكسر الباء والميم على ان يكون محققاً من بسم الله و اما بفتحهها على ان يكون مصدراً جعلياً منه كالحوقلة من لاحول ولاقوة الا بالله و الهيللة من «لا اله الا الله»، كذا قيل. ولا يخفى ان قرائة ذلك بالتاء كما هو المتداول فى الالسن والمرسوم فى الكتب تنا في الوجه الأول اذ عليه لابد وان يقرء بالهاء كما لا يخفى (محمد على)
The word 'basmala' is either read with kasra of the ba' and meem, so that it is a mimetic word from 'bismi Allah', or with fatha of both, so that it is a derived verbal noun from it, like 'hawqala' from 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa bi-llah' and 'haylala' from 'la ilaha illa Allah'. Thus it is said. And it is not hidden that reading it with 'ta' as is common on tongues and written in books is problematic on the first view, because on that view it must be read with 'ha' as is not hidden. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a brief grammatical and lexicographical note on the word 'basmala' itself. The commentator presents two possible pronunciations and derivations: (1) with kasra on the ba' and meem, treating it as a mimetic word (muhqaqq) coined from the phrase 'bismi Allah'; (2) with fatha on both, making it a derived verbal noun (masdar ja'li) from the same phrase, analogous to other Arabic coinages like 'hawqala' from the phrase 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa bi-llah' and 'haylala' from 'la ilaha illa Allah'. The commentator notes that the common practice of writing 'basmala' with a 'ta' (تاء) at the end is problematic on the first view, because if it is a mimetic word, it should end with the letter 'ha' (هاء) instead, as is obvious.

Key Terms
بسملة
محقق
مصدر جعلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) With a ha' (ه)
C) With an alif (ا)
D) With a ya' (ي)

Key Takeaway
The pronunciation and spelling of 'basmala' depend on whether it is a mimetic word or a derived verbal noun, with implications for how it is written in Islamic texts.
