Grammatical Parsing of 'Mansub' as Object of Reason
Commentator explains the accusative case of 'mansub' as an object of reason and the original forms of 'khayr' and 'sharr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical function of an accusative noun as an object of reason (maf'ul li-ajlih) in Arabic and recognize the original forms of 'khayr' and 'sharr'.

Core Idea
The accusative can indicate reason; and 'khayr' and 'sharr' originally had hamza.

This passage from the commentator teaches us two things: first, how to parse an accusative noun as an object of reason (maf'ul li-ajlih), and second, a philological note about the original forms of the words 'khayr' (good) and 'sharr' (evil). These insights are directly useful for reading classical Arabic and understanding grammatical nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusative noun
Find the word that is marked as accusative (mansub). In this passage, it is 'mansub' itself? Actually the passage begins by saying 'mansub' (accusative) because it is an object of reason for 'iftataha' (he opened). So the word being discussed is presumably the word in the main text that is accusative. The student should note that the commentator is explaining the reason for the accusative case.
2
Recognize the grammatical function: maf'ul li-ajlih
The commentator states that this accusative is an object of reason (maf'ul li-ajlih). This term refers to the reason or purpose for the action expressed by the verb. The student should understand that this construction is used to explain why an action occurred.
Example: 'I studied for knowledge' where 'knowledge' is the object of reason.
3
Examine the original forms of 'khayr' and 'sharr'
The commentator notes that 'al-khayr' originally is 'akhayr' with a hamza, similar to 'al-sharr' originally 'asharr'. These forms are rarely used except with the hamza deleted. The student should see that classical Arabic sometimes preserves older forms in certain contexts.
This is a philological observation about sound changes.
4
Identify examples of the original forms
The commentator gives examples: 'Bilal is the best of people' (using 'akhayr'?), 'ibn al-akhayr', and a Qur'anic recitation of 'al-asharr'. The student should note that these are specific instances where the original hamza is retained.
This shows that the original forms are not entirely extinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منصوب على انه مفعول له لقوله افتتح والخير أصله أخير كما ان الشر اصله آشر لكنهما لا يكاد ان يستعملا الا محذوفا الهمزه ومما جاء به على الاصل ، بلال خير الناس وابن الاخير و من الكذاب الاشتر في قرانة بعضهم (محمد على)
Accusative because it is an object of reason for his saying 'ifta-hta' (he opened). And 'al-khayr' (good) originally is 'akhayr', just as 'al-sharr' (evil) originally is 'asharr', but they are hardly ever used except with the hamza deleted. And among what comes in the original form is: 'Bilal is the best of people' and 'ibn al-akhayr' and 'from the worst liar' in the recitation of some of them. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical note on the accusative form 'mansub' (accusative) in the phrase. The commentator identifies it as an object of reason (maf'ul li-ajlih) for the verb 'ifta-hta' (he opened). He then digresses to explain that the word 'khayr' (good/ better) originally came as 'akhayr' with a hamza, similar to 'sharr' (evil/ worse) originally 'asharr'. However, these forms are almost always used without the hamza, except in a few cases like the proper name 'Bilal' in the phrase 'Bilal is the best of people' (implying the original form is retained in some readings), 'ibn al-akhayr' (a known figure), and a recitation of the Qur'an by some where 'al-asharr' appears.

Key Terms
مفعول له
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-khayar
B) Akhayr
C) Khayrun
D) Al-akhayr

Key Takeaway
The accusative case can indicate the reason for an action (maf'ul li-ajlih), and words like 'khayr' and 'sharr' originally had hamza that is often dropped.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why both basmala and hamdala are best speech
Commentator explains why both basmala and hamdala are best speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the commentator's reasoning for why both the basmala and hamdala qualify as the best speech (khayr al-kalam).

Core Idea
Both basmala and hamdala are best speech because they mention Allah, the noblest existent.

This brief commentary addresses why the author of the matn says to begin with the best speech. The commentator clarifies that both the basmala (bismi Allah) and the hamdala (al-hamdu lillah) fall under this description. The shared reason is that both phrases contain the mention of Allah, who is the noblest being, encompassing all realms of existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being explained
The matn states: 'beginning with the best speech.' The commentator is explaining what qualifies as 'best speech' and why.
2
Extract the commentator's reason
The commentator says both basmala and hamdala are from the best speech because 'they contain mention of the noblest of existents' (li-ih-tiwa'ihima 'ala dhikr ashraf al-mawjudat).
3
Understand the scope of 'noblest existents'
The phrase 'ashraf al-mawjudat' includes celestial and terrestrial, immaterial and material beings. This emphasizes that Allah is the most noble of all existents, regardless of realm.

قوله ابتداء بخير الكلام فان كلاً من البسملة و الحمدلة من خير الكلام لاحتوائهما على ذكر اشرف الموجودات العلوية والسفلية المجردة والمادية (التقريب ص ۶)
His saying: beginning with the best speech, for each of the basmala and the hamdala is from the best speech because they contain mention of the noblest of existents, both celestial and terrestrial, immaterial and material. (al-Taqrib p.6)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a brief explanation of why the phrase 'best speech' (khayr al-kalam) applies to both the basmala and the hamdala. The reason is that both phrases contain the mention of Allah, who is the noblest of all existents, encompassing both the higher (celestial) and lower (terrestrial) realms, as well as immaterial (mujarrada) and material (maddiyya) beings. Thus, opening a book with either of these constitutes beginning with the best speech.

The Hadith on Beginning Important Matters with Praise of Allah
Commentator cites multiple versions of the hadith about beginning significant matters with praise of Allah or mentioning Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three variants of the hadith on beginning important matters and explain the meaning of 'abtar' as used in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Core Idea
An important matter not begun with Allah's praise is 'abtar'—severed from blessing.

This lesson explores a foundational hadith that shapes the opening of Islamic speeches, writings, and scholarly works. The commentator cites three versions and clarifies the key term 'abtar' by quoting Ibn al-Athir. Understanding this hadith equips you to recognize the proper Islamic etiquette for commencing any significant undertaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three variants
Read the three hadith versions: one with 'bi-hamd Allah' (praise), one with 'bi-dhikr Allah' (remembrance), and one with 'bi-ism Allah' (name of Allah). Note the common structure: 'Every matter of importance...' and the conclusion '...abtar'.
The variants show the principle is broad—any form of beginning with Allah is commendable.
2
Understand the term 'abtar'
The commentator quotes Ibn al-Athir defining 'abtar' as 'aqta' (severed, cut off). The root 'b-t-r' implies cutting or truncation. In the hadith, it metaphorically means the matter lacks completeness and blessing.
This lexical clarification is crucial for interpreting the hadith's warning.
3
Apply to historical example
The commentator mentions Ziyad's sermon called 'al-batra'' because it omitted praise of Allah and blessings on the Prophet. Reflect on why this omission renders the sermon incomplete according to the hadith.
This example illustrates the practical implementation of the hadith in Islamic tradition.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Recognize that this hadith underlies the obligatory or recommended opening formulas in khutbahs, books, and legal documents. Scholars rely on such hadiths to establish the sunnah of beginning with Allah's name and praise.
Knowing these variants helps verify the authenticity and scope of the practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بحديث خير الانام روى عن النبي (ص) انه قال كل امر ذي بال لا يبدو فيه بحمد الله فهو ابتر. و روى ايضاً عنه (ص): «كل أمر لا يبدو فيه بذكر الله فهو ابتر». و روى عنه (ص) ايضاً : كل امر خطير ذى بال لا يبدو فيه باسم الله فهو ابتر قال ابن الاثير في النهاية فهو ابتر اى اقطع والبتر القطع و فى حديث زياد انه قال في خطبته البتراء كذا، قيل: انها اء لانه لم يذكر فيها ((الله) عزوجل ولا صلى فيها على النبي (ص). (التقريب فى المنطق ص ۶)
His saying: by the hadith of the best of mankind. It is narrated from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'Every matter of importance that is not begun with praise of Allah (bi-hamd Allah) is severed (abtar).' And it is also narrated from him: 'Every matter that is not begun with remembrance of Allah (bi-dhikr Allah) is severed.' And it is also narrated from him: 'Every serious matter of importance that is not begun with the name of Allah (bi-ism Allah) is severed.' Ibn al-Athir said in al-Nihaya: 'abtar' means severed (aqta'), and 'batar' is cutting. And in the hadith of Ziyad, he said in his 'abtar' sermon, it was said that it is called 'abtar' because he did not mention Allah Almighty nor send blessings upon the Prophet (PBUH). (al-Taqrib fi al-Mantiq p.6)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a citation of a well-known hadith about beginning important matters. The commentator provides three variants: one using 'bi-hamd Allah' (with praise of Allah), one using 'bi-dhikr Allah' (with remembrance of Allah), and one using 'bi-ism Allah' (with the name of Allah). He explains the meaning of 'abtar' (severed) by quoting Ibn al-Athir, who defines it as 'cut off' (aqta'). He then gives an example of a sermon called 'al-batra' by Ziyad, which was so named because it lacked mention of Allah and blessings on the Prophet. This serves to underscore the importance of beginning with Allah's name and praise.

Key Terms
ابتر
الخطبة البتراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The hadith encourages beginning every important matter with mention of Allah, and the term 'abtar' signifies a lack of divine blessing and completion.

Kufan vs Basran: Repeating the Preposition with Cojoined Pronouns
Commentator discusses the difference between Kufan and Basran grammarians on repeating the preposition when a pronoun is cojoined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Kufan and Basran positions on repeating the preposition when a genitive pronoun is cojoined and apply the Basran rule.

Core Idea
Basrans required repetition; Kufans allowed omission due to differing views on pronoun-preposition attachment strength.

In Arabic grammar, when you conjoin a pronoun that is in the genitive case after a preposition, a question arises: must you repeat the preposition? The Basran school insisted on repetition, while the Kufans (along with some others) permitted omission. This lesson explains the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the construction
Find a sentence where a genitive pronoun is cojoined to another genitive pronoun after a preposition. Example: 'marartu bihi wa biha' vs 'marartu bihi wa ha'.
The cojoined pronoun is in the genitive case due to the preposition.
2
State the Basran position
Basrans required repeating the preposition for any cojoined genitive pronoun.
They argued that the pronoun's attachment to the preposition is too strong to allow omission.
3
State the Kufan position
Kufans, along with al-Akhfash, Yunus, and Ibn Malik, allowed omission, viewing the pronoun's attachment as not absolute.
They permitted omission, considering the conjunction sufficient.
4
Apply the rule
Given a sentence, determine whether it follows Basran or Kufan grammar. In formal written Arabic, the Basran rule is more common.
Practice by analyzing Quranic examples or classical prose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عطف على الضمير المجرور في «عليه على ما جوزه الكوفيون والاخفش ويونس و ابن مالك من عليم وجوب اعادة الخافض و اما البصريون فقد وجبوا ذلك وقالوا: ان اتصال الضمير المجرور بالجار اشد
Conjunction to the pronoun that is genitive (majrur) in 'alayhi' (upon him) according to what the Kufans, al-Akhfash, Yunus, and Ibn Malik permitted, from those who know the necessity of repeating the preposition. As for the Basrans, they made that necessary and said: the connection of the genitive pronoun with the preposition is stronger.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical note on the rule of conjointing a pronoun that is in the genitive case after a preposition. The commentator mentions that the Kufan school, along with certain grammarians like al-Akhfash, Yunus, and Ibn Malik, allowed omitting the repetition of the preposition when a pronoun is cojoined to another that already has a preposition. However, the Basran school required that the preposition be repeated, arguing that the pronoun's attachment to the preposition is too strong to allow omission. The commentator implies that the Basran view is more rigorous.

Two Types of Preposing and Resolving Contradictions
Author distinguishes two types of preposing and resolves an apparent contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of preposing (taqdim) in Arabic grammar and apply this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in classical grammatical analyses.

Core Idea
Preposing in Arabic is not a single phenomenon—intention determines whether it is type 1 or type 2.

In Arabic grammatical analysis, contradictions sometimes appear when different scholars use the same term 'taqdim' for different operations. This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali clarifies that preposing can be either with the intention of delaying the syntactic function or without that intention. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting grammatical rulings and avoiding confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of taqdim
Read the passage carefully and list the two types: preposing with intention of delay and preposing without intention of delay. Note the Arabic terms: تقديم على نية التأخير and تقديم لا على نية التأخير.
The passage gives examples: preposing the predicate over the subject is type 1; preposing the subject over the predicate is type 2.
2
Apply the distinction to the example of 'al-hamd'
The passage discusses the preposing of 'al-hamd' in 'al-hamdu lillahi' and notes that one scholar (Sahib al-Kashshaf) meant type 1, while another meant type 2. Determine which type applies to 'al-hamd' based on the context given.
The author asserts that according to one interpretation, 'al-hamd' is preposed without intention of delay (type 2), which resolves the contradiction.
3
Analyze the additional explanation
The final sentence offers an alternative analysis: originally it was 'ahmadu Allaha hamdan' then changed, making the preposing of 'al-hamd' type 1. Compare both analyses.
This step shows that even within the same word, different analyses can lead to different typings, but both are logically consistent once the type is specified.
4
Practise with new examples
Take other common Arabic sentences (e.g., 'Zaydun qāma', 'Qāma Zaydun', 'al-ḥamdu lillāhi') and classify the preposing as type 1 or type 2.
For each example, ask: Is the fronted element intended to be delayed in its syntactic function (type 1) or is it naturally the first element (type 2)?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت: قد اجاب المصنف عن ذلك في شرح التلخيص : بان التقديم ضربان تقديم على نية التأخير كتقديم الخبر على المبتداء وتقديم لا على نية التأخير كتقديم المبتداء على الخير وذلك بان تعمد الى اسم فتقدمه تارة على الفعل فتجعله مبتداء نحو زيد قام وتؤخره تارة فتجعله فاعلاً نحو قام زيد و تقديم الحمد من الضرب الثانى و مراد صاحب الكشاف ثمة هو الضرب الاول فلا تنافى وكلامه ايضاً مشحون باطلاق التقديم على الضرب الاول انتهى . و قد يقال ان الاصل احمد الله حمداً، فعدل من النصب الى الرفع لما ذكر، ثم قدم الحمد على الظرف فيكون ح من الضرب الأول فلا يلزم محذور اصلاً فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی)
I said: The author has answered that in his commentary on al-Talkhis: that preposing is of two types: preposing with intention of delay, like preposing the predicate over the subject; and preposing without intention of delay, like preposing the subject over the predicate. That is, you intentionally take a noun and sometimes front it over the verb, making it a subject, as in 'Zayd stood'; and sometimes you delay it, making it a predicate, as in 'stood Zayd'. The preposing of 'al-hamd' is of the second type, whereas the intention of the author of al-Kashshaf there is the first type, so there is no contradiction. Also his speech is replete with applying preposing to the first type. End. And it may be said: the original is 'I praise God with praise', then it was changed from accusative to nominative for the reason mentioned, then 'al-hamd' was proposed over the adverb, so it becomes from the first type, so no problem occurs at all. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection about the preposing of 'al-hamd' in the phrase 'al-hamdu lillahi'. The author explains that preposing (taqdim) in Arabic is of two types. The first type is preposing with the intention of delaying the syntactic function: for example, fronting the predicate (khabar) before the subject (mubtada'), like 'Zaydun qaama' (Zayd stood) where 'Zayd' is the subject but is preposed. The second type is preposing without that intention, like fronting the subject before the predicate, as in 'Zaydun qaama' (Zayd stood) where 'Zayd' is subject and is naturally first. The author of al-Kashshaf meant the first type when he used 'taqdim' for the preposing of 'al-hamd', but the comment by the author (sahib) being discussed intended the second type, so there is no contradiction. An additional note suggests that the original construction was 'ahmadu Allaha hamdan' (I praise God with praise), then it was changed to nominative and 'al-hamd' was fronted over the adverb, making it of the first type, thus avoiding any problem. The commentary is a typical expansion_probe, clarifying a subtle grammatical point by distinguishing the two senses of taqdim.

Key Terms
تقديم على نية التأخير
تقديم لا على نية التأخير
ضربان
نية التأخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The distinction between preposing with and without intention of delay is a precise tool that resolves apparent contradictions in Arabic grammatical analysis.

The Logical Utility of 'Ahad' in Surah Ikhlas
Author defends the usefulness of the predicate 'Ahad' against the charge of vain attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three non-redundant interpretations of the divine name 'Ahad' and explain why the predication is informative.

Core Idea
'Ahad' is not tautological; it negates divisibility, precedence, or peerhood.

The verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' might seem to simply repeat that Allah is one. But logicians and commentators have shown that the predicate 'Ahad' carries specific meanings that are not identical to the subject 'Allah'. In this passage, the commentator defends the utility of this predication by showing three distinct interpretations, each adding information about God's nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Understand the claim that the predication would be vain if 'Ahad' simply repeats 'Allah'. This objection is based on a certain reading of the pronoun.
2
Reinterpret the Pronoun
The author offers an alternative parsing where 'huwa' refers to the Lord mentioned in the dialogue, making 'Ahad' a second predicate or substitute, thus not redundant.
3
Extract the Three Meanings
From the text, list the three possible meanings of 'Ahad': not divisible (indivisible), first (as in Sunday), and peerless. Each is distinct from the name 'Allah'.
4
Assess Informativeness
For each meaning, confirm that it adds new information about God's attributes, thus the predication is beneficial and not vain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لزوم كون الحمل لغواً فى حيز المنع ايضاً، لان مبناه على ان يكون الضمير (اي: ضمير هو في «قل و الله احد للشأن و الله احد مبتداء و خبراً مفسراً له و هو غير لازم الجواز ان يقدر الضمير عايداً للرب المذكور فى كلام قريش لماروى انهم قالوا للرسول (ص): «يا محمد صف لاربك الذي تدعونا اليه فنزلت الآية وأمر (ص) بان يقول لهم هواى ربى الذى ادعوكم اليه الله أحد. فاحد بدل او خبرثان، ولو سلم فنقول : ليس معنى احد انه واحد لا اثنان بل بمعنى انه غير مبعض ولا يجزى كما ورد عليه الرواية عن ابن عباس او بمعنى اوّل كما يقال يوم الاحد أو بمعنى لا نظير له كما يقال فلان واحد ای فرد لا نظير له وعلى كل واحد منها يكون الحمل مفيداً كما لا يحق (من المحمد على)
And the necessity of the attribution being vain is also in the realm of being prevented, because it is based on the pronoun (i.e., the pronoun 'huwa' in 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad') being for the matter, and 'Allahu Ahad' being a subject and predicate explaining it. But this is not necessary, because it is permissible to take the pronoun as referring to the Lord mentioned in the speech of Quraysh, as it was narrated that they said to the Messenger (s): 'O Muhammad, describe your Lord whom you call us to,' so the verse was revealed, and he (s) was commanded to say to them: 'He is my Lord whom I call you to, Allah is Ahad.' So 'Ahad' is either a substitute or a second predicate. And even if we grant, we say: The meaning of 'Ahad' is not that He is one not two, but rather that He is not divisible nor partitioned, as narrated from Ibn Abbas, or it means 'first' as in 'Yawm al-Ahad' (Sunday), or it means 'without peer' as is said 'so-and-so is one (wāḥid)', i.e., an individual with no peer. And on each of these meanings, the attribution is beneficial, as is not hidden (from Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad'. The author responds to an objection that the predicate 'Ahad' would be tautological or vain if the verse is taken as a simple equation (He is Allah, He is one). He shows that the pronoun 'huwa' can refer to the Lord mentioned in the prior dialogue (the question of Quraysh), not as a pronoun of the matter. Then 'Ahad' is an additional predicate or substitute. Even if one insists on the pronoun of the matter, 'Ahad' has several meanings (indivisible, first, peerless) that are not identical to 'Allah', so the attribution is informative. This is a typical logical exercise in tafsir, showing the utility of definitions and the avoidance of circularity.

Key Terms
حمل (ḥaml): Attribution or predication; here the logical connection of the predicate 'Ahad' to the subject.
لغو (laghw): Vain, pointless; an attribution that adds no new information.
أحد (aḥad): A divine name interpreted here as 'indivisible', 'first', or 'peerless', not simply numerical one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The predicate 'Ahad' in Surah Ikhlas adds specific negations of divisibility, precedence, and peerhood, making the attribution informative.

Connotation of Proper Names in Islamic Logic
Author illustrates how proper names connote qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze how proper names carry connoted qualities in Islamic rhetorical and argumentative contexts.

Core Idea
Proper names can connote qualities, not just identify individuals.

In Islamic logic and rhetoric, words often carry more than their primary reference. This lesson explores how proper names—like 'Hatim' for generosity and 'Musa' for truth—acquire connoted meanings through widespread cultural association. Understanding this phenomenon is crucial for interpreting figurative language and arguments in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proper names in the passage
Locate the four proper names mentioned: Hatim, Musa, Pharaoh, and Abu al-Hasan. Each is a proper name that primarily identifies a specific individual.
Note that 'Abu al-Hasan' is a kunya (teknonym) for Ali ibn Abi Talib.
2
Extract the connoted quality for each name
The passage states that each name indicates a quality: Hatim → generosity, Musa → being with truth, Pharaoh → being with falsehood, Abu al-Hasan → decisive judgment.
These connotations arise from historical or legendary associations, not from the names' linguistic roots.
3
Understand the mechanism of connotation
The author explains that due to widespread familiarity, these names are sometimes used to refer to those qualities themselves, not just the individuals. This is a form of metonymy.
In Islamic logic, this is discussed under 'dalāla' (indication), where secondary meanings can attach to words.
4
Analyze the proverbs as evidence
The passage cites two proverbs: 'Every Pharaoh has a Musa' and 'A case has no Abu al-Hasan'. These show how proper names are deployed to make general statements about qualities (falsehood meets truth; a case lacks decisive judgment).
These proverbs are used in Islamic discourse to argue about patterns in history and morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك كدلالة لفظ «حاتم» على الجود ولفظ «موسى» على المحقية ولفظ «فرعون» على المبطلية ولفظ ابى الحسن» على الفيصلية. و من هذا ربما يطلق و يراد بها المعانى المشتهرة بها قالوا لكل فرعون موسى» و «قضية لا ابا حسن لها». (میرزا محمد علی)

And from this, it is sometimes used and intended by them the common meanings for which they are known. They said: 'Every Pharaoh has a Musa' and 'A case has no Abu al-Hasan (i.e., no decisive judge)'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion discussing how proper names (a‘lām) can connote certain attributes due to widespread association. Examples: 'Hatim' is known for generosity, 'Musa' for truth (against Pharaoh), 'Pharaoh' for falsehood, 'Abu al-Hasan' (a title of ‘Ali) for decisive judgment. The author notes that sometimes these names are used to refer to those qualities themselves, as in proverbs like 'Every Pharaoh has a Musa' (every falsehood has a truth) and 'A case has no Abu al-Hasan' (a case without a decisive judgment). This shows that in Islamic logic and rhetoric, proper names can function as descriptive terms, which is relevant to understanding figurative language and argumentation.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalāla) – indication, connotation
علم (‘alam) – proper name

Grammatical Debate on 'Ayy al-Tafsiriyya': Appositive or Coordinate?
Debate on 'ayy' as appositive or coordinate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the grammatical debate on whether 'ayy' introduces an appositive or coordinate construction and identify the key evidence from Arabic grammatical theory.

Core Idea
Grammarians treat appositives like adjectives; since pronouns cannot have adjectives, they cannot have appositives.

In this passage, the hashya author enters a long-standing debate about the explanatory particle 'ayy'. The question is whether the phrase after 'ayy' is an appositive (عطف بيان) or a coordinate construction (عطف نسق). The author defends al-Sakkaki's minority view by appealing to a principle of Arabic grammar: that appositives function like adjectives, and just as a pronoun cannot be described by an adjective, it cannot have an appositive. This logical analogy carries significant weight for interpreting Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The majority say 'ayy' introduces an appositive; al-Sakkaki says it introduces a coordinate conjunction. Note the key figures: the majority vs. the author of al-Miftah.
2
Extract the evidence for the majority
The author of Sharh al-Talkhis (al-Qazwini) argues that 'ayy' can explain a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition, and an accusative pronoun without emphasis. This suggests it is appositive.
3
Analyze the counter-evidence for al-Sakkaki
The hashya author points out that grammarians equate appositives with adjectives. Since pronouns cannot be described (لا ينعت الضمير), they cannot have appositives. Therefore, when 'ayy' explains a pronoun, it must be coordinate.
4
Apply to a Quranic example
Ibn Hisham criticized al-Zamakhshari for allowing an appositive on the pronoun in verse 5:117. Verify why: if 'an' clause were appositive to the pronoun, it would violate the rule. This example illustrates the practical consequence of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «اى الايصال»: اختلف العلماء فى ان ما بعد «اى» التفسيرية هل هو عطف بيان لما قبلها أو عطف نسق ؟ والجمهور على الاول وصاحب المفتاح ومن تبعه على الثاني. قال المصنف في شرح التلخيص و وقوعها تفسيراً للضمير المجرور من غير اعادة الجار والفسير المتصل المرفوع من غير تأكيد او فصل يقوى مذهب الجمهور». و فيه ان تلك الخصوصية و ان كانت تقويه، لكن مجرد وقوعها تفسيراً للضمير يقوى مذهب «السكاكي» بناء على ما نص عليه التحويون من ان عطف البيان في الجوامد بمنزلة النعت في المشتقات. فكما ان الضمير لا ينعت كذلك لا يعطف عليه عطف بيان و لذا عاب ابن هشام على الزمخشري في تجويزه فى قوله تعالى: «ما قلت لهم الا ما أمرتني به ان اعبدوا الله ربي وربكم...» (سورة مائدة آية (۱۱۷) ان يكون (ان» مصدرية وهى وصلتها عطف بيان على الهاء في به. (محمد علی)
His saying: 'i.e., al-īṣāl': The scholars differed regarding what follows the explanatory 'ayy' – whether it is an appositive (ʻaṭf bayān) of what precedes it or a coordinating conjunction (ʻaṭf nasaq). The majority hold the former, while the author of al-Miftah (al-Sakkaki) and his followers hold the latter. The author said in Sharh al-Talkhis: 'Its occurrence as an explanatory for a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition, and [as explanatory] for a connected accusative pronoun without emphasis or separation, strengthens the majority view.' But there is: that specific [characteristic] even if it strengthens it, the mere occurrence as an explanatory for the pronoun strengthens the position of al-Sakkaki, based on what the grammarians have stated: that appositives (ʻaṭf bayān) for concrete nouns are like adjectives (naʻt) for derived nouns. So just as a pronoun cannot be described, likewise an appositive cannot be appended to a pronoun. Therefore Ibn Hisham criticized al-Zamakhshari for permitting, in His saying: 'I did not say to them except what You commanded me: that you worship Allah, my Lord and your Lord' (5:117) that 'an' be a masdariyya and its clause be an appositive to the pronoun 'hu' in 'bihi'.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on a grammatical point concerning the explanatory particle 'ayy' (i.e., meaning 'that is'). The issue is whether the phrase after 'ayy' is an appositive (ʻaṭf bayān) or a coordinating conjunction (ʻaṭf nasaq). The majority say it is an appositive, but al-Sakkaki (author of al-Miftah) and his followers say it is coordinating. The author of Sharh al-Talkhis (al-Qazwini) supported the majority view with the observation that 'ayy' can explain a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition, and explain a connected accusative pronoun without emphasis. However, the hashya author (Muhammad Ali) argues that this very fact actually supports al-Sakkaki, because grammarians state that appositives for concrete nouns are like adjectives for derived nouns, and since pronouns cannot receive adjectives, they also cannot have appositives. He cites Ibn Hisham's criticism of al-Zamakhshari for allowing an appositive on a pronoun in a Quranic verse. This discussion shows the depth of Arabic grammatical analysis intertwined with logical interpretation of texts.

Key Terms
أي التفسيرية
عطف بيان
عطف نسق
ضمير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the hashya author support al-Sakkaki's view that 'ayy' introduces a coordinate construction?
A) Because the majority view is weak.
B) Because pronouns cannot take appositives, just as they cannot take adjectives.
C) Because al-Zamakhshari made a mistake.
D) Because 'ayy' is always followed by a verb.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'ayy' depends on whether the following element is a noun or pronoun; pronouns cannot have appositives, a principle derived from the analogy between appositives and adjectives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following sentence: 'جاء زيد أي الرجل الكريم'. Determine whether 'الرجل الكريم' is an appositive or coordinate. Justify using the rule from the lesson.
?
In Q. 5:117, why did Ibn Hisham reject al-Zamakhshari's parsing? Explain the logical connection between the appositive-as-adjective analogy and the pronoun restriction.

Bara'at al-Istihlal: The Art of the Opening
Definition and application of bara'at al-istihlal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bara'at al-istihlal and identify its role in evaluating the suitability of a text's opening to its subject matter.

Core Idea
The opening's brilliance is measured by its suitability to the content.

In this passage, the commentator defines 'bara'at al-istihlal' as a rhetorical device. It explains the linguistic and technical meanings, and how it relates to the author's choice of wording. Understanding this concept helps you appreciate how classical scholars assessed the coherence of a text's beginning with its overall theme.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the line in the Arabic where 'براعة الاستهلال' is explicitly defined. Notice that it is introduced after discussing 'الاول اولى'.
The definition appears around the phrase 'والمراد من البراعة براعة الاستهلال'.
2
Linguistic breakdown
Identify the root words: 'برع' (to surpass) and 'استهل' (to cry at birth). Understand how the literal meaning 'superiority of the beginning' is derived.
The commentator emphasizes the metaphorical extension from crying at birth to the start of anything.
3
Technical definition
Extract the technical meaning: 'كون الديباجة مناسبة للمقصود' (the appropriateness of the preamble to the intended subject). Note the example given of grammar book preambles mentioning 'رفع' and 'نصب'.
The commentator explains that it is a cause (سبب) but named after the effect (مسبب) to indicate perfection in causality.
4
Application to the text
See how the commentator uses bara'at al-istihlal to argue that the general interpretation (عموم) of 'الاول اولى' yields manifest brilliance for both logic and theology, unlike the specific interpretation.
This shows the practical use of the concept in evaluating textual openings.

(٤٢) قوله « والاول اولى»: اى كون المراد نفس الأمر عموماً هو الاولى لأنه يحصل عليه البراعة الظاهرة بالنسبة الى قسمى الكتاب اعنى المنطق والكلام بخلاف ما اذا كان المراد به خصوص ملة الاسلام اذ لا يحصل البراعة الظاهرة ح الا بالنسبة الى قسم واحد من قسمى الكتاب وهو الكلام. فان قلت انما قيد البراعة بالظاهرة وكانه يحصل البراعة بالنسبة الى القسمين على الثاني ايضاً لكنها لا تكون ظاهرة ؟ قلت نعم فان المنطق من مقدمات الكلام لاحتياجه اليه في ترتيب الأقيسة و انتاج النتائج ولذا قدمه المصنف عليه، هذا. ولا يذهب عليك ان المذكور في تلك الرسالة هو القسم الاول خاصة افرده المحشي بالشرح. ثم المراد من البراعة براعة الاستهلال والبراعة مصدر برع الرجل اذا فاق اقرانه واترابه والاستهلال مصدر استهل الصبي اذا صاح عند الولادة، ثم استعير لاول كل شيء فبراعة الاستهلال بحسب المعنى اللغوى تفوق الأول، و في الاصطلاح كون الديباجة مناسبة للمقصود كان يذكر في ديباجة كتب النحو مثلاً «الرفع» و «النصب» و «الجر» وغير ذلك مما يبحث فيه عنه وهو في التحقيق سبب لتفوق الابتداء لكنه سمى باسم المسبب تنبيهاً على كماله في السببية (ميرزا محمد علی) (٤٣) یعنى قوله ((كنا))، فانهم ربما يطلقونه (الظرف) و يريدون به الجار والمجرور تشبيهاً لهما به في عدم الاستقرار والاحتياج الى المتعلق. ولان كثيراً من المجرورات ظروف زمانية او مكانية فاطلق الظرف على مجموع المجرورات تجوزاً فعلى هذا فالمراد بالظرف المجرور خاصة. ثم انهم ربما يطلقون الجار والمجرور و يريدونها معاً لكنها اذا اطلقا معاً يراد بالجار الجار، وبالمجرور، المجرور كما ان لفظى الفقير و المسكين كذلك وفي ذلك الغز بعضهم الفقير والمسكين كالجار والمجرور اذا افترقا اجتمعا و اذا اجتمعا افترقا . (محمد على) قوله « الظرف اما متعلق بجعل ...» بان يكون معمولاً له بحسب المحل اذ هو في محل النصب على معنى أن الفعل يقتضى نصبه لوكان متعدياً اليه بنفسه. فان قلت: يقع في عبارة بعضهم الجار يتعلق بكذا و في عبارة بعضهم الجار والمجرور وفى بعضهم المجرور فيما هو المحرر من هذه العبارات. قلت التحقيق ان العامل اما يعمل فى الاسم الذى يلى الجارلا فى الجار و اطلاق من قال: «العامل في الجار» تسامح و قول من قال: «الجار والمجرور يتعلق بكذا ملموح فيه ان الجار والمجرور ينزل منزلة من المجرور فجعل المتعلق لهما معاً، والحق ما ذكرناه آخراً كذا ذكره الامام الحديثي في شرح الحاجبية. (عبد الرحيم) (٤٤) سورة البقرة الآية ٢٢ (٤٥) جواب عما يرد على التوجيه الثانى اعنى تعلق الظرف برفيق من انه مضاف اليه و معمول المضاف اليه لا يجوز ان يتقدم على المضاف على ما بين في النحو. و حاصل الجواب ان المنع انما هو فيما لم يكن المعمول من الظروف و اما اذا كان منها فلا يمنع لا تساعهم فيها ما لم يتسع في غيرها. (میرزا محمد علی) (٤٦) قوله : والاول اقرب لفظاً يعنى ان تعلقه يجعل اقرب من حيث اللفظ من تعلقه برفيق وابعد من حيث المعنى. أما الأول فلكونه سالماً من شايبة تقديم معمول المضاف اليه على المضاف فانه و ان كان جايزاً عند بعضهم في امثال ما نحن فيه، لكنه خلاف الاصل فان مرتبة المعمول بعد مرتبة العامل ومن التزام الحذف و التفسير ايضاً على ما ذكرناه فانه ايضاً خلاف الاصل بخلاف تعلقه برفيق فانه لابد وان يلتزم فيه بواحد منهما والا لما يكون صحيحاً من أصله . و اما الثانى فلما فيه من شايبة ان جعله تعالى معلل بالعباد وقد قالوا ان افعال الله تعالى لا تعلل بالاغراض و ان اشتملت على منافع و غايات لا تحصى بل بالحكم و المصالح. وهذا و ان كان ضعيفاً سخيفاً كما ستعرف في اواخر الكتاب لكن الحمل على ما يصح عند الجميع أولى من الحمل على ما لا يصح عند البعض كما لا يخفى. وقيل : لانه يصير المعنى ح هكذا: «الحمد لله الذى جعل التوفيق خير رفيق لانتفاعنا فان اللام كما ذكر المحشي للانتفاع ولا يخفى ما فيه من سوء الادب بخلاف مالو تعلق برفيق كما لا يخفى. وقد يعلل : بانه يكون المعنى انه تعالى جعل لانتفاعنا التوفيق خير رفيق» ولا يعلم منه ان مرافقة ح التوفيق لهم أو لغيرهم. إذ يجوز ان يجعل التوفيق رفيقاً لغيرهم من الأصدقاء والاحباء ويكونون منتفعين بذلك كما
(42) His statement 'and the first is more appropriate': meaning that the intended meaning being the general aspect of the matter is more appropriate because it achieves the manifest brilliance (bara'a) in relation to both parts of the book, namely logic and theology, unlike if the intended meaning is specifically the Milla of Islam, for then the manifest brilliance is only achieved for one part of the book, which is theology. If you say: he qualified 'brilliance' as 'manifest', and it seems that brilliance is obtained for both parts on the second interpretation as well, but it is not manifest? I say: yes, because logic is a premise of theology due to its need for arranging syllogisms and producing conclusions, so the author placed it before theology. This. And let it not escape you that what is mentioned in that treatise is the first part only; the commentator devoted his commentary to it. Then the intended meaning of 'brilliance' is the brilliance of the opening (bara'at al-istihlal). 'Brilliance' is the verbal noun of 'bara'a' meaning he surpassed his peers, and 'istihlal' is the verbal noun of 'istahalla' meaning the baby cries at birth. Then it was metaphorically applied to the beginning of everything. So 'bara'at al-istihlal' linguistically means the superiority of the beginning, and technically it means the appropriateness of the preamble to the intended subject, as one mentions in preambles of grammar books, for example, 'raf', 'nasb', 'jar', etc., about which it investigates. It is in reality a reason for the superiority of the beginning, but it is named after the effect to indicate its perfection in causality. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (43) That is, his statement 'kana': for sometimes they use 'the adverb' (zarf) and mean the preposition and its object (jar and majrur), likening them to it in lacking stability and needing attachment. And because many objects of prepositions are temporal or spatial adverbs, so 'zarf' is metaphorically applied to the set of objects. Thus here the intended meaning of 'zarf' is the object specifically. Then sometimes they use 'jar and majrur' together, but when used together, 'jar' means the preposition and 'majrur' means the object, just as the words 'faqir' and 'miskin' are likewise. And in that debate some say 'faqir' and 'miskin' are like 'jar' and 'majrur': when separated they are together and when together they are separate. (Muhammad Ali) His statement 'the zarf is either attached to ja'ala...' meaning it is its object in terms of grammatical case, since it is in the accusative case, indicating that the verb requires its object if it were transitive to it directly. If you say: in some statements, 'jar is attached to such-and-such', in others 'jar and majrur', and in others 'majrur'—what is correct among these expressions? I say: the truth is that the governing word acts on the noun that follows the jar, not on the jar. The statement of one who says 'the governing word acts on the jar' is lenient. The statement of one who says 'jar and majrur are attached to such-and-such' implies that the jar and majrur are treated as one unit, so the attachment is for both together. And the truth is what we mentioned last. Thus the Imam al-Hadithi mentioned it in his commentary on al-Hajibiyya. (Abd al-Rahim) (44) Surah al-Baqarah verse 22. (45) An answer to what is directed against the second interpretation, i.e., attaching the zarf to 'rafiq', that it is a possessive construction and the object of the possessive is not allowed to precede the possessor as established in grammar. The summary of the answer: the prohibition applies when the object is not an adverb, but if it is an adverb, it is not prohibited because they allow expansion in their case, even if they do not allow it in other cases. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (46) His statement: 'and the first is closer in wording' means that its attachment is closer in wording and farther in meaning. As for the first, because it is free from the suspicion of advancing the object of the possessive over the possessive, even if it is permissible by some in examples like ours, it is contrary to the default. The rank of the object is after the rank of the governor, and there is also the commitment to ellipsis and interpretation as we mentioned, which is also contrary to the default. Unlike its attachment to 'rafiq', for one must commit to one of these two, otherwise it would not be correct at all. As for the second, because there is the suspicion that it makes the Almighty's action motivated by servants, and they have said that the actions of Allah are not motivated by purposes (aghrad), even if they encompass countless benefits and ends, but rather by wisdom and interests. And even if this (objection) is weak and flimsy, as you will know at the end of the book, still, to interpret in a way that is valid for everyone is better than interpreting in a way that is not valid for some, as is not hidden. And it is said: because the meaning becomes like this: 'Praise be to Allah who made tawfiq a good companion for our benefit, for the lam as the commentator mentioned is for benefit, and there is no hiding what bad adab it contains, unlike if it is attached to 'rafiq', as is not hidden. And it is argued that the meaning becomes: 'He made tawfiq a good companion for our benefit', but it is not known from this whether tawfiq's companionship is for them or for others. For it is possible that He made tawfiq a companion for others, such as friends and loved ones, and they benefit from that, as...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a hashya refining the interpretation of the opening line of the book: 'الحمد لله الذي جعل التوفيق خير رفيق' (Praise be to Allah who made success the best companion). The author first discusses the meaning of 'الاول اولى' in the context of the author's phrase, preferring a general interpretation that yields evident brilliance for both logic and theology. He explains that logic is a premise for theology. Then he defines 'براعة الاستهلال' (brilliance of opening) linguistically and technically. Next, the commentator turns to grammatical issues: the word 'ظرف' (adverb) is used here to refer to the prepositional phrase (جار ومجرور) by analogy. He clarifies the usage patterns of these terms. Then he addresses the attachment of the prepositional phrase: should it be attached to 'جعل' (made) or to 'رفيق' (companion)? Two possibilities are considered. The objection involves the rule that the object of a possessive cannot precede the possessor in the regular order. The answer is that adverbs (ظروف) are an exception because they are flexible. Finally, the author prefers the first attachment (to 'جعل') because it avoids theological impropriety – if attached to 'رفيق', it could imply that Allah's action is motivated by the benefit of the servants, which is not proper for divine actions. He notes that although this objection is weak, it is safer to choose the interpretation that is acceptable to all. The passage demonstrates meticulous attention to both grammatical precision and theological correctness in classical commentary.

البراعة
الاستهلال

Key Takeaway
Bara'at al-istihlal is a key rhetorical device measuring how well a text's opening hints at its content, blending linguistic and technical dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Grammatical Terminology: Zarf, Jar, and Majrur
Clarification of grammatical terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the technical uses of 'zarf', 'jar', and 'majrur' in classical Arabic grammar and identify the metaphorical extension of 'zarf' to include prepositional phrases.

Core Idea
The term 'zarf' can be extended to cover the whole prepositional phrase due to similarity in lacking stability.

This passage clarifies that 'zarf' (adverb) is sometimes used metaphorically to refer to the prepositional phrase (jar and majrur). Both share the property of grammatical dependence. Understanding this terminology is crucial for parsing complex sentences in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms
Find the words 'ظرف' and 'جار ومجرور' in the Arabic passage. Note the context: the commentator is explaining the usage of these terms.
The discussion begins with 'قوله ((كنا))' and then explains that 'ظرف' is used to mean the whole prepositional phrase.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage says they liken the prepositional phrase to an adverb in lacking stability (عدم الاستقرار) and needing an attachment (تعلق). This is the basis for the metaphorical extension.
The term 'ظرف' originally means a container or adverb of time/place, but here it is used more broadly.
3
See the usage patterns
Observe how when 'جار ومجرور' are used together, each retains its specific meaning: 'جار' is the preposition, 'مجرور' is the object. The commentator compares them to 'فقير ومسكين' which have a similar relationship.
The phrase 'اذا افترقا اجتمعا واذا اجتمعا افترقا' describes the distribution of meanings.
4
Apply to the context
In the sentence 'الظرف اما متعلق بجعل', determine what 'الظرف' refers to. According to the passage, it refers to the prepositional phrase (المجرور خاصة), not just an adverb.
This clarifies the intended meaning in the grammatical analysis of the opening line.

(محمد على) قوله « الظرف اما متعلق بجعل ...» بان يكون معمولاً له بحسب المحل اذ هو في محل النصب على معنى أن الفعل يقتضى نصبه لوكان متعدياً اليه بنفسه. فان قلت: يقع في عبارة بعضهم الجار يتعلق بكذا و في عبارة بعضهم الجار والمجرور وفى بعضهم المجرور فيما هو المحرر من هذه العبارات. قلت التحقيق ان العامل اما يعمل فى الاسم الذى يلى الجارلا فى الجار و اطلاق من قال: «العامل في الجار» تسامح و قول من قال: «الجار والمجرور يتعلق بكذا ملموح فيه ان الجار والمجرور ينزل منزلة من المجرور فجعل المتعلق لهما معاً، والحق ما ذكرناه آخراً كذا ذكره الامام الحديثي في شرح الحاجبية. (عبد الرحيم) (٤٤) سورة البقرة الآية ٢٢ (٤٥) جواب عما يرد على التوجيه الثانى اعنى تعلق الظرف برفيق من انه مضاف اليه و معمول المضاف اليه لا يجوز ان يتقدم على المضاف على ما بين في النحو. و حاصل الجواب ان المنع انما هو فيما لم يكن المعمول من الظروف و اما اذا كان منها فلا يمنع لا تساعهم فيها ما لم يتسع في غيرها. (میرزا محمد علی) (٤٦) قوله : والاول اقرب لفظاً يعنى ان تعلقه يجعل اقرب من حيث اللفظ من تعلقه برفيق وابعد من حيث المعنى. أما الأول فلكونه سالماً من شايبة تقديم معمول المضاف اليه على المضاف فانه و ان كان جايزاً عند بعضهم في امثال ما نحن فيه، لكنه خلاف الاصل فان مرتبة المعمول بعد مرتبة العامل ومن التزام الحذف و التفسير ايضاً على ما ذكرناه فانه ايضاً خلاف الاصل بخلاف تعلقه برفيق فانه لابد وان يلتزم فيه بواحد منهما والا لما يكون صحيحاً من أصله . و اما الثانى فلما فيه من شايبة ان جعله تعالى معلل بالعباد وقد قالوا ان افعال الله تعالى لا تعلل بالاغراض و ان اشتملت على منافع و غايات لا تحصى بل بالحكم و المصالح. وهذا و ان كان ضعيفاً سخيفاً كما ستعرف في اواخر الكتاب لكن الحمل على ما يصح عند الجميع أولى من الحمل على ما لا يصح عند البعض كما لا يخفى. وقيل : لانه يصير المعنى ح هكذا: «الحمد لله الذى جعل التوفيق خير رفيق لانتفاعنا فان اللام كما ذكر المحشي للانتفاع ولا يخفى ما فيه من سوء الادب بخلاف مالو تعلق برفيق كما لا يخفى. وقد يعلل : بانه يكون المعنى انه تعالى جعل لانتفاعنا التوفيق خير رفيق» ولا يعلم منه ان مرافقة ح التوفيق لهم أو لغيرهم. إذ يجوز ان يجعل التوفيق رفيقاً لغيرهم من الأصدقاء والاحباء ويكونون منتفعين بذلك كما
(42) His statement 'and the first is more appropriate': meaning that the intended meaning being the general aspect of the matter is more appropriate because it achieves the manifest brilliance (bara'a) in relation to both parts of the book, namely logic and theology, unlike if the intended meaning is specifically the Milla of Islam, for then the manifest brilliance is only achieved for one part of the book, which is theology. If you say: he qualified 'brilliance' as 'manifest', and it seems that brilliance is obtained for both parts on the second interpretation as well, but it is not manifest? I say: yes, because logic is a premise of theology due to its need for arranging syllogisms and producing conclusions, so the author placed it before theology. This. And let it not escape you that what is mentioned in that treatise is the first part only; the commentator devoted his commentary to it. Then the intended meaning of 'brilliance' is the brilliance of the opening (bara'at al-istihlal). 'Brilliance' is the verbal noun of 'bara'a' meaning he surpassed his peers, and 'istihlal' is the verbal noun of 'istahalla' meaning the baby cries at birth. Then it was metaphorically applied to the beginning of everything. So 'bara'at al-istihlal' linguistically means the superiority of the beginning, and technically it means the appropriateness of the preamble to the intended subject, as one mentions in preambles of grammar books, for example, 'raf', 'nasb', 'jar', etc., about which it investigates. It is in reality a reason for the superiority of the beginning, but it is named after the effect to indicate its perfection in causality. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (43) That is, his statement 'kana': for sometimes they use 'the adverb' (zarf) and mean the preposition and its object (jar and majrur), likening them to it in lacking stability and needing attachment. And because many objects of prepositions are temporal or spatial adverbs, so 'zarf' is metaphorically applied to the set of objects. Thus here the intended meaning of 'zarf' is the object specifically. Then sometimes they use 'jar and majrur' together, but when used together, 'jar' means the preposition and 'majrur' means the object, just as the words 'faqir' and 'miskin' are likewise. And in that debate some say 'faqir' and 'miskin' are like 'jar' and 'majrur': when separated they are together and when together they are separate. (Muhammad Ali) His statement 'the zarf is either attached to ja'ala...' meaning it is its object in terms of grammatical case, since it is in the accusative case, indicating that the verb requires its object if it were transitive to it directly. If you say: in some statements, 'jar is attached to such-and-such', in others 'jar and majrur', and in others 'majrur'—what is correct among these expressions? I say: the truth is that the governing word acts on the noun that follows the jar, not on the jar. The statement of one who says 'the governing word acts on the jar' is lenient. The statement of one who says 'jar and majrur are attached to such-and-such' implies that the jar and majrur are treated as one unit, so the attachment is for both together. And the truth is what we mentioned last. Thus the Imam al-Hadithi mentioned it in his commentary on al-Hajibiyya. (Abd al-Rahim) (44) Surah al-Baqarah verse 22. (45) An answer to what is directed against the second interpretation, i.e., attaching the zarf to 'rafiq', that it is a possessive construction and the object of the possessive is not allowed to precede the possessor as established in grammar. The summary of the answer: the prohibition applies when the object is not an adverb, but if it is an adverb, it is not prohibited because they allow expansion in their case, even if they do not allow it in other cases. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (46) His statement: 'and the first is closer in wording' means that its attachment is closer in wording and farther in meaning. As for the first, because it is free from the suspicion of advancing the object of the possessive over the possessive, even if it is permissible by some in examples like ours, it is contrary to the default. The rank of the object is after the rank of the governor, and there is also the commitment to ellipsis and interpretation as we mentioned, which is also contrary to the default. Unlike its attachment to 'rafiq', for one must commit to one of these two, otherwise it would not be correct at all. As for the second, because there is the suspicion that it makes the Almighty's action motivated by servants, and they have said that the actions of Allah are not motivated by purposes (aghrad), even if they encompass countless benefits and ends, but rather by wisdom and interests. And even if this (objection) is weak and flimsy, as you will know at the end of the book, still, to interpret in a way that is valid for everyone is better than interpreting in a way that is not valid for some, as is not hidden. And it is said: because the meaning becomes like this: 'Praise be to Allah who made tawfiq a good companion for our benefit, for the lam as the commentator mentioned is for benefit, and there is no hiding what bad adab it contains, unlike if it is attached to 'rafiq', as is not hidden. And it is argued that the meaning becomes: 'He made tawfiq a good companion for our benefit', but it is not known from this whether tawfiq's companionship is for them or for others. For it is possible that He made tawfiq a companion for others, such as friends and loved ones, and they benefit from that, as...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a hashya refining the interpretation of the opening line of the book: 'الحمد لله الذي جعل التوفيق خير رفيق' (Praise be to Allah who made success the best companion). The author first discusses the meaning of 'الاول اولى' in the context of the author's phrase, preferring a general interpretation that yields evident brilliance for both logic and theology. He explains that logic is a premise for theology. Then he defines 'براعة الاستهلال' (brilliance of opening) linguistically and technically. Next, the commentator turns to grammatical issues: the word 'ظرف' (adverb) is used here to refer to the prepositional phrase (جار ومجرور) by analogy. He clarifies the usage patterns of these terms. Then he addresses the attachment of the prepositional phrase: should it be attached to 'جعل' (made) or to 'رفيق' (companion)? Two possibilities are considered. The objection involves the rule that the object of a possessive cannot precede the possessor in the regular order. The answer is that adverbs (ظروف) are an exception because they are flexible. Finally, the author prefers the first attachment (to 'جعل') because it avoids theological impropriety – if attached to 'رفيق', it could imply that Allah's action is motivated by the benefit of the servants, which is not proper for divine actions. He notes that although this objection is weak, it is safer to choose the interpretation that is acceptable to all. The passage demonstrates meticulous attention to both grammatical precision and theological correctness in classical commentary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the term 'zarf' used to refer to the prepositional phrase?
A) Because prepositions are always adverbs
B) Because the prepositional phrase lacks stability and needs attachment, like adverbs
C) Because the grammarians made a mistake
D) Because it is the standard term in grammar

Key Takeaway
In classical grammar, 'zarf' can be extended to include the full prepositional phrase by analogy, important for accurate parsing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the Quranic sentence 'إياك نعبد', identify the grammatical role of 'إياك' and explain whether it is a jar or majrur, using the terminology from this lesson.

Commentary Notes
advanced
10 min
Resolving Grammatical Ambiguity with Theological Considerations
Preference for attachment to 'ja'ala' over 'rafiq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate grammatical alternatives by considering both linguistic rules and theological principles, as demonstrated in the choice between attaching the prepositional phrase to 'ja'ala' or to 'rafiq'.

Core Idea
Grammatical choices can have theological implications; the safest interpretation avoids attributing purpose to divine actions.

The passage presents two possible attachments for the prepositional phrase in the opening line. The commentator prefers attaching to 'ja'ala' because attaching to 'rafiq' could imply that Allah's action is motivated by human benefit, which contradicts theological principles. This lesson shows how grammatical analysis must be guided by sound creed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two attachments
Find where the passage mentions the two possibilities: attaching the prepositional phrase to 'جعل' or to 'رفيق'. The text says 'الظرف اما متعلق بجعل...' and then discusses the alternative.
The context is the analysis of 'الحمد لله الذي جعل التوفيق خير رفيق'.
2
Understand the grammatical objection
The objection to attachment to 'رفيق' is that it would be an object of a possessive (as 'رفيق' is in possessive construction with 'خير'), and the rule states that the object of a possessive cannot precede the possessor in the default word order.
The commentator notes that this rule is generally strict unless exceptions apply.
3
Consider the exception for adverbs
The answer to the objection is that adverbs (ظروف) are an exception to this rule because grammarians allow greater flexibility with them. Therefore, attachment to 'رفيق' is grammatically possible.
This shows that the rule is not absolute.
4
Examine the theological objection
Even though attachment to 'رفيق' is grammatically possible, it is theologically problematic. If the phrase means 'made tawfiq a good companion for our benefit', it attributes a purpose (اللام للانتفاع) to Allah's action, implying He acts for the sake of creation.
The passage states that scholars have said Allah's actions are not motivated by purposes (أغراض) but by wisdom (حكم ومصالح).
5
See the preference
The commentator prefers attachment to 'جعل' because it avoids the theological problem, even though it requires ellipsis (حذف) and is less direct grammatically. He concludes that 'الاول اقرب لفظاً و أبعد معنى'.
This demonstrates prioritizing theological soundness over grammatical simplicity.

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a hashya refining the interpretation of the opening line of the book: 'الحمد لله الذي جعل التوفيق خير رفيق' (Praise be to Allah who made success the best companion). The author first discusses the meaning of 'الاول اولى' in the context of the author's phrase, preferring a general interpretation that yields evident brilliance for both logic and theology. He explains that logic is a premise for theology. Then he defines 'براعة الاستهلال' (brilliance of opening) linguistically and technically. Next, the commentator turns to grammatical issues: the word 'ظرف' (adverb) is used here to refer to the prepositional phrase (جار ومجرور) by analogy. He clarifies the usage patterns of these terms. Then he addresses the attachment of the prepositional phrase: should it be attached to 'جعل' (made) or to 'رفيق' (companion)? Two possibilities are considered. The objection involves the rule that the object of a possessive cannot precede the possessor in the regular order. The answer is that adverbs (ظروف) are an exception because they are flexible. Finally, the author prefers the first attachment (to 'جعل') because it avoids theological impropriety – if attached to 'رفيق', it could imply that Allah's action is motivated by the benefit of the servants, which is not proper for divine actions. He notes that although this objection is weak, it is safer to choose the interpretation that is acceptable to all. The passage demonstrates meticulous attention to both grammatical precision and theological correctness in classical commentary.

الظرف
المضاف إليه
اللام للانتفاع

Active vs Passive Reading of 'Huda'
Author explains why 'huda' is better read as passive rather than active to fit the context of description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the verbal noun 'huda' is used in an active or passive sense based on grammatical and contextual clues.

Core Idea
The active reading makes the Prophet a receiver; the passive makes him a means of guidance.

In Islamic theological discourse, the precise meaning of words like 'huda' (guidance) can shift based on grammatical analysis. This passage from a classical commentary shows how the context of description (maqam al-na't) guides the choice between active and passive interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word under analysis
The word 'huda' (هدى) appears in a context describing the Prophet. Notice that it is treated as a verbal noun (masdar).
The term 'masdar mabni lil-maf'ul' indicates it is built for the passive voice.
2
Understand the two possible constructions
If built for the active, it means 'accepting guidance' (qubul al-hidaya). If built for the passive, it means 'a means by which one is guided' (muhtada bihi).
The active shifts focus to the Prophet as recipient; the passive to him as instrument.
3
Assess contextual suitability
The context of description (na't) demands a meaning that portrays the Prophet as a guide, not a guided one. The passive meaning fits; the active does not.
The passage explicitly states: 'the active would not be suitable for the context of description'.
4
Consider the exception
If an underlying prepositional phrase (jar wa majrur) is estimated—e.g., 'he is the one deserving to be guided by'—then the active becomes acceptable because the guidance referred to is that of others, not the Prophet himself.
This exception shows the interplay between grammar and intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مصدر مبنى للمفعول : لأنه لو كان مبنياً للفاعل يكون بمعنى قبول الهداية فلا يكون مناسباً لمقام النعت بخلاف ما لو كان مبنياً للمفعول فانه يكون بمعنى مهتدى به و هذا المعنى مناسب للمقام، هذا اذا لم يقدر فى الكلام جار ومجرور واما اذا قدر بان يكون التقدير: «هو الاهتداء به حقيق فيصح ان يكون مبنياً للفاعل مع مناسبته للمقام لان المراد من الهداية ح ليس هدايته (ص) بل هداية غيره من الانام (عبدالرحيم)
His saying: 'a verbal noun built for the passive' because if it were built for the active, it would mean 'accepting guidance' and thus would not be suitable for the context of description, unlike if it were built for the passive, for it would then mean 'a means by which one is guided', and this meaning is suitable for the context. This is if no prepositional phrase is estimated in the speech. But if it is estimated such that the estimation is 'he is the one deserving to be guided by', then it is valid for it to be built for the active with its suitability to the context, because the intended meaning of guidance here is not his guidance (peace be upon him) but the guidance of others among mankind. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage clarifies why the word 'huda' (guidance) in the text is interpreted as a verbal noun built for the passive (mabni lil-maf'ul). The author argues that if it were built for the active, it would mean 'accepting guidance' (qubul al-hidaya), which is not appropriate for describing the Prophet in that context. Instead, the passive meaning ('a means by which one is guided') fits better. However, the author adds an exception: if we estimate a deleted prepositional phrase (jar wa majrur), such that the underlying phrase is 'he is the one deserving to be guided by' (huwa al-ihtida' bihi haqiq), then the active form becomes acceptable. This is because the guidance referred to is not the Prophet's own guidance but his guidance of others. Thus, the passage shows careful grammatical reasoning to align with theological appropriateness.

مهتدى به
قبول الهداية

Evaluating Grammatical Attachments for Theological Correctness
Author argues that the phrase 'bi'l-iqtida' should modify 'iqtida' not 'yaliq' for correct meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the attachment of a prepositional phrase in Arabic (ta'alluq) and determine which attachment yields a theologically sound meaning in a given context.

Core Idea
Grammatical attachment must align with theological propriety: what is becoming to the Prophet vs. to us.

In Islamic texts, even grammatical choices can carry profound theological implications. This passage from a commentary on a hadith text demonstrates how the attachment of a prepositional phrase (bi'l-iqtida) affects the meaning and must be chosen to avoid attributing imperfection to the Prophet. You will learn to apply similar reasoning when interpreting Arabic phrases in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible attachments
Locate the prepositional phrase 'bi'l-iqtida' and note that it could attach either to the verb 'yaliq' or to the verbal noun 'iqtida'. The Arabic text explicitly considers both possibilities.
The author states the target attachment directly: 'attached to al-iqtida' not to yaliq'.
2
Determine the meaning of each attachment
If attached to 'yaliq', the phrase 'bi'l-iqtida' functions as the object of suitability: 'our following is becoming to him'. If attached to 'iqtida', it functions as an adverbial: 'our following him is becoming to us'. Translate each attachment to see the difference.
The theological nuance hinges on who receives honor – the Prophet or us.
3
Evaluate theological acceptability
Consider the implications: attaching to 'yaliq' implies the Prophet gains honor from our following, which is inappropriate for his already perfect status. Attaching to 'iqtida' implies we gain honor from following him, which is theologically sound.
This step uses 'urf and theological principles to judge appropriateness.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objector argues that when attached to 'iqtida', the verb 'yaliq' has an implicit object that could be 'us' or 'him', so no clear preference. The author responds that the undesirable consequence (attributing imperfection to the Prophet) is certain only on the first interpretation, and contextual clues point to 'us'.
The author uses a principle of avoiding certainty of harm (daf' al-darar al-muhaqqaq).
5
Apply the reasoning to other cases
Practice by finding another Arabic phrase where two possible attachments yield different theological or legal meanings, and determine which is correct based on contextual clues and avoidance of problematic implications.
This step generalizes the tool beyond the given passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله متعلق بالاقتداء لا بـ « يليق» وذلك لانه لو تعلق به لكان المعنى ان اقتدائنا يليق به، يصير كمالاً و جاهاً له (ص) بخلاف مالو تعلق بالاقتداء فان المعنى ح : ان اقتدائنا به يليق بنا انه يكون شرفاً وعزة لنا وهذا معنى قوله (ره): «فان اقتدائنا به انما يليق بنا لا به ... فان قلت اذا قدر متعلقاً بالاقتداء يكون قوله: «يليق» مطلقاً يحتمل ان يكون معموله المحذوف بمعنى بمعنى انه (continues on next page)
His saying: 'attached to 'al-iqtida'' not to 'yaliq', because if it were attached to it, the meaning would be that our following (iqtida') is becoming to him, becoming perfection and honor for him (peace be upon him). Unlike if it were attached to 'al-iqtida', then the meaning is that our following him is becoming to us, it being honor and glory for us. And this is the meaning of his saying (may God have mercy on him): 'For our following him is only becoming to us, not to him...' If you say: when it is estimated as attached to 'al-iqtida', his saying 'yaliq' is absolute, it is possible that its deleted object is the word 'bina' (to us) or the word 'bihi' (to him), and that estimation is not preferred on the basis that estimating the deleted from the same genus as the mentioned is better, and the general does not indicate the specific, so what is mentioned is not a reason for preferring attachment to 'al-iqtida' as is apparent. I say: though it suffices in the context of preference that the occurrence of the problematic consequence on the estimation of attachment to 'yaliq' is certain, while on the latter it is not certain. Indeed, the external and contextual clues indicate that the estimated word is 'bina' not 'bihi', so it is preferred over it and the desired is established without any objection. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical dispute about the attachment of the prepositional phrase 'bi'l-iqtida' (by following). The author argues that it should be attached to the verbal noun 'iqtida' (following) rather than to the verb 'yaliq' (becoming). The reason is theological: if it were attached to 'yaliq', it would imply that our following the Prophet is becoming to him, granting him perfection and honor, which is inappropriate because the Prophet already has perfect honor. If attached to 'iqtida', it means that our following him is becoming to us, bringing us honor. The author anticipates an objection: when it is attached to 'iqtida', the verb 'yaliq' has an implicit object that could be either 'us' or 'him', and there is no clear indicator to prefer one. The author responds that the undesirable consequence (i.e., attributing imperfection to the Prophet) is avoided only on the second attachment, and external clues (our context) show that the intended object is 'us'. Thus, the correct attachment is established.

Distinguishing Hasr and Ikhtisas in Taqdim al-Zarf
Author critiques the view that taqdim al-zarf indicates hasr, distinguishing between hasr and ikhtisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hasr and ikhtisas according to the definitions of Taqi al-Din al-Subki, and identify the grounds for the objection against equating them.

Core Idea
Hasr negates all others; ikhtisas merely focuses on one without negation—a distinction many miss.

In Arabic syntax, fronting a circumstantial adverb often prompts questions about whether it indicates restriction or mere specification. This passage from a hashia clarifies the precise difference between hasr and ikhtisas, while also confronting a common objection that the two terms are synonymous. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyse definitional precision in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Locate the Arabic terms الحصر and الاختصاص in the passage. Notice how the author uses them in contrast.
The terms appear early in the quotation from Taqi al-Din al-Subki.
2
Extract the definition of hasr
According to al-Subki, hasr is 'negation of what is not mentioned and affirmation of what is mentioned'. This implies exclusive restriction.
Hasr is a stronger claim: it excludes all alternatives.
3
Extract the definition of ikhtisas
Ikhtisas is 'intending the external thing in its particularity without addressing the negation of anything else'. It is specification without exclusivity.
Ikhtisas merely highlights a particular, without denying others.
4
Analyze the objection to the distinction
The commentator objects that the author's claim that 'distinguished scholars did not mention hasr' is refuted because the present commentator himself uses the word hasr. Moreover, the common view holds that hasr and ikhtisas are synonyms.
The objection is both terminological and conceptual: the author's own words undermine his position.
5
Evaluate the strength of the objection
The commentator admits that the claim of ikhtisas in fronting is only predominant, not universal. This weakens a strict distinction, as even the author concedes exceptions.
Assess whether the objection successfully refutes al-Subki's definitional distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله تقديم الظرف لقصد الحصر: قد اشتهر كلام الناس في أن تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص وقد يفهم كثير من الناس من الاختصاص الحصر وليس كذلك وإنما الاختصاص شيء والحصر شيء آخر والفضلاء لم يذكروا ذلك الحصر وإنما اعتبروا بالاختصاص. والفرق بينهما أن الحصر نفي غير المذكور وإثبات المذكور والاختصاص قصد الخارجي من جهة خصوصه من غير تعرض لنفي غيره قاله التقى الدين السبكي. وقوله الفضلا لم يذكروا في ذلك لفظ الحصر، برده ما قاله المحشى في هذا المقام و غير واحد من ذوي الأفهام و ما ذكره من التفرقة بين الحصر والاختصاص خلاف المشهور، فإن المشهور أنها مترادفان، ثم ادعاء الاختصاص في تقديم المعمول ليس كلياً بل أغْلَبِي فاعتراض ابن الحديد بقوله تعالى: «كلا هديناه ونوحاً هدينا ليس بشيء... (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: 'The introduction of the circumstantial adverb for the purpose of restriction (hasr)': The people's discourse has become well-known that fronting the governed term conveys specification (ikhtisas), and many people understand from specification restriction, but it is not so. Indeed specification is one thing and restriction is another. The distinguished scholars did not mention 'restriction' in that regard, they only considered specification. The difference between them is that restriction is the negation of what is not mentioned and the affirmation of what is mentioned, while specification is the intention of the external thing in terms of its particularity without addressing the negation of anything else. This was said by Taqi al-Din al-Subki. And his saying: 'The distinguished scholars did not mention the word restriction in that,' is refuted by what the commentator said in this context and many others with understanding. What he mentioned of the distinction between restriction and specification is contrary to the well-known view, for the well-known view is that they are synonymous. Then the claim of specification in fronting the governed term is not universal but predominant. So the objection of Ibn al-Hadid with His statement: 'Each We guided, and Noah We guided before' is nothing... (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a previous claim that the fronting of a circumstantial adverb (like 'بنا' in 'بنا يليق') is done for the purpose of restriction (hasr). The commentator (al-Muhashi) first reports a common distinction: fronting a governed term (like the object) indicates specification (ikhtisas), and many mistakenly equate specification with restriction. He then quotes Taqi al-Din al-Subki's explicit definition: 'restriction' is the negation of everything not mentioned and the affirmation of what is mentioned, whereas 'specification' is merely intending a particular entity without concern for excluding others. The commentator then objects that the author's earlier statement that 'the distinguished scholars did not mention restriction' is refuted by what the present commentator (al-Muhashi) himself says—because he is using the word 'restriction' in this very passage. Moreover, the commonly accepted view is that restriction and specification are synonymous, so the distinction he is drawing is contrary to the consensus. He further weakens his own position by admitting that the claim of specification in fronting is not universal but only predominant. Therefore, Ibn al-Hadid's objection citing the Qur'anic verse 'كلا هديناه ونوحاً هدينا' (where 'نوحاً' is fronted but does not indicate restriction) is baseless. The passage ends abruptly, but the commentator has made his point: the distinction is invalid or at least not well-established.

Key Terms
الحصر
الاختصاص
تقديم الظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hasr affirms only the mentioned and negates all else; ikhtisas indicates particularity without negation.
