The Derivation of 'Āl' from 'Ahl' – Evidence from Diminutives
Rebuttal of derivational claim using diminutive evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the evidence from diminutive forms to determine the derivational origin of a word in Arabic.

Core Idea
The diminutive 'uwail' proves 'āl' is not directly from 'ahl'.

In this lesson, we examine a key linguistic argument from the super-commentary. The claim that 'āl' derives from 'ahl' via two heart-transpositions is challenged by evidence from the diminutive forms. By analyzing the utterances of a Bedouin as reported by al-Kisā'ī, we can determine the true derivational relationship between these two words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The super-commentator begins by stating an objection: someone claims that 'ahīl' is the diminutive of 'āl', implying 'āl' derives from 'ahl' via heart-transposition. Find this claim in the Arabic.
The objection is in the first sentence: 'لانسلم ان اهيلاً بالهاء تصغير «آل»'.
2
Examine the evidence from al-Kisā'ī
The super-commentator cites al-Kisā'ī who reports hearing an eloquent Bedouin say four words: 'āl', 'uwail', 'ahl', 'ahīl'. Notice the pairing: 'āl' with 'uwail' and 'ahl' with 'ahīl'.
This indicates that 'uwail' is the diminutive of 'āl', and 'ahīl' is the diminutive of 'ahl'.
3
Distinguish the diminutives
The Bedouin used 'uwail' for the diminutive of 'āl' and 'ahīl' for the diminutive of 'ahl'. This shows that 'āl' and 'ahl' have different roots and are not directly related via simple transformation.
If 'āl' were derived from 'ahl', its diminutive would be 'ahīl', but it is not.
4
Conclude the derivational relationship
Since 'āl' has its own diminutive 'uwail', it is not derived from 'ahl' through two heart-transpositions. The super-commentator refutes the claim and implies that 'āl' is a separate root.
This is a critical refinement: the earlier claim is rejected based on linguistic evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لقائل ان يقول انا لانسلم ان اهيلاً بالهاء تصغير «آل» حتى يكون دليلاً عليه، لم لا يجوز ان يكون تصغير اهل بالهاء؟ وقد نقل عن الكسائى انه قال: سمعت اعرابياً فصيحاً يقول: «آل» و «أويل» و «اهل» و «اهيل» وهو نص فى ان اهيلا تصغير اهل وان تصغير آل أويل بالواو فلا وجه ح للقول بان اصل آل اهل والارتكاب بالقلب مرتين فافهم (ميرزا محمد علی). قوله خص استعماله في الاشراف اى في العقلاء الذين لهم خطر عظيم، جمع شريف وهو فعيل من الشرف محركة بمعنى العلو والمكان العالى تشبيهاً للعلو المعنوى بالعلو المكاني وفي الحديث: اذا اتيكم شريف قوم فاكرموه سئل و ما الشريف ؟ فقال : الشريف من كان له مال، قلت فالحسيب ؟ قال : الذي يفعل الافعال الحسنة بماله وغير ماله. هذا. والحاصل ان الآل اخص من الاهل مطلقا من جهة ان الاهل يعم العقلاء وغيرهم ، يقال : اهل الرجل لما له وعياله ، والال يخص العقلاء فقط و ايضاً هو يعم من العقلاء من له خطر وغيره والآل لا يستعمل الا فيمن له خطر دنيوياً او مطلقاً كآل فرعون و آل محمد (ص) ، قيل لما ارتكبوا في الآل التغيير اللفظى بتغيير الهاء ارتكبوا التخصيص الاول توقياً للملائمة بين اللفظ والمعنى ولما كان الهاء حرفاً ثقيلاً لكونه من اقصى الحلق تطرق الى الكلمة بسبب قلبها الى الالف الذى هو حرف خفيف - نقص قوى فارتكبوا التخصيص الثاني جبراً لهذا النقص. (محمد على)
One may object: we do not accept that 'ahīl' with hā' is the diminutive of 'āl' so that it would be evidence for it; why may it not be the diminutive of 'ahl' with hā'? It has been transmitted from al-Kisā'ī that he said: I heard a eloquent Bedouin say: 'āl', 'uwail', 'ahl', 'ahīl' – this is a clear text that 'ahīl' is the diminutive of 'ahl' and that the diminutive of 'āl' is 'uwail' with wāw, so there is no basis for the claim that the origin of 'āl' is 'ahl' and that it involves two heart-transpositions. So understand. (Mīrzā Muḥammad ʿAlī). His statement: 'Its usage is specific to nobles' – i.e., to rational beings who have great stature, plural of sharīf which is faʿīl from sharaf (with fatḥa) meaning elevation and high place, likening spiritual elevation to physical elevation. In the hadith: 'When the noble of a people comes to you, honor him.' He was asked: Who is the noble? He said: The noble is one who has wealth. I said: Who is the ḥasīb? He said: One who does good deeds with his wealth and without it. This. The conclusion: 'āl' is more specific than 'ahl' absolutely, in that 'ahl' includes rational and non-rational; one says 'ahl of a man' for his property and family, while 'āl' is only for rational beings. Also, among rational beings, 'āl' includes only those with stature, whereas 'ahl' is general. Hence 'āl' is only used for one with worldly or absolute stature, like Āl Firʿawn and Āl Muḥammad (ṣ). It is said: since they performed verbal alteration in 'āl' by changing the hā', they performed the first specification to ensure harmony between word and meaning; and since the hā' is a heavy letter because it is from the deepest part of the throat, when it was turned into alif – a light letter – the word suffered a loss of strength, so they performed the second specification to compensate for this loss. (Muḥammad ʿAlī)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by a super-commentator (Mīrzā Muḥammad ʿAlī) on two points: the derivation of the word 'āl' and its usage. The first part rebuts an objection that 'ahīl' might be the diminutive of 'ahl' rather than 'āl'. The commentator cites al-Kisā'ī's report of a Bedouin using both diminutives distinctly, proving that 'ahīl' is the diminutive of 'ahl' and 'uwail' is the diminutive of 'āl'. Therefore, the claim that 'āl' originates from 'ahl' via two heart-transpositions is false. The second part explains that 'āl' is exclusively used for rational beings of high status, unlike 'ahl' which includes non-rational entities like property. The author analogizes spiritual elevation to physical height and quotes a hadith about honoring a noble person. Finally, a rationale is given for why 'āl' was both altered from 'ahl' and restricted in meaning: the change of the heavy 'hā' to light 'alif' weakened the word, so the semantic restriction compensated. This is a typical hashiya exercise in lexicological justification.

Key Terms

أهل
تصغير

Checkpoint

A) 'Āl' is derived from 'ahl'.
B) 'Āl' is an independent root.
C) 'Ahl' is derived from 'āl'.
D) 'Īl' is the original form.

Key Takeaway
The diminutive form of a word provides crucial evidence for its root; 'uwail' shows 'āl' is not derived from 'ahl'.

The Semantic Restriction of 'Āl' to Rational Beings of High Status
Explanation of the semantic restriction of 'āl' to nobles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the semantic scopes of 'āl' and 'ahl' and explain the phonetic rationale for 'āl's restriction to nobles.

Core Idea
'Āl' is for nobles only; 'ahl' is for everyone and everything; the restriction compensates for phonetic weakening.

After establishing the derivational independence of 'āl', the super-commentator explains its specialized usage. Unlike 'ahl', which can refer to family, property, and all rational beings, 'āl' is reserved for rational beings of high status. The author even provides a philosophical justification: the phonetic change from heavy 'hā' to light 'alif' weakened the word, so the semantic restriction added strength.

Guided Path
1
List the meanings of 'ahl'
The text states that 'ahl' can mean a man's property, family, and rational beings in general. Write down these categories.
'Ahl' is broader, including non-rational things like property.
2
List the meanings of 'āl'
The text specifies that 'āl' is only used for rational beings who have great stature, like Āl Firʿawn and Āl Muḥammad (ṣ).
'Āl' excludes non-rational and low-status rational beings.
3
Understand the rationale for restriction
The super-commentator explains that the phonetic change (hā' to alif) weakened the word, so the semantic restriction compensates. This is a case of linguistic economy.
The heavy hā' is from the deepest throat, while alif is light; the loss of weight required added semantic specificity.
4
Apply the hadith example
The hadith about honoring a noble (sharīf) is quoted; the sharīf is defined as one with wealth. This illustrates the type of person to whom 'āl' refers.
The hadith shows that 'sharīf' implies worldly stature, which matches 'āl's usage.

One may object: we do not accept that 'ahīl' with hā' is the diminutive of 'āl' so that it would be evidence for it; why may it not be the diminutive of 'ahl' with hā'? It has been transmitted from al-Kisā'ī that he said: I heard a eloquent Bedouin say: 'āl', 'uwail', 'ahl', 'ahīl' – this is a clear text that 'ahīl' is the diminutive of 'ahl' and that the diminutive of 'āl' is 'uwail' with wāw, so there is no basis for the claim that the origin of 'āl' is 'ahl' and that it involves two heart-transpositions. So understand. (Mīrzā Muḥammad ʿAlī). His statement: 'Its usage is specific to nobles' – i.e., to rational beings who have great stature, plural of sharīf which is faʿīl from sharaf (with fatḥa) meaning elevation and high place, likening spiritual elevation to physical elevation. In the hadith: 'When the noble of a people comes to you, honor him.' He was asked: Who is the noble? He said: The noble is one who has wealth. I said: Who is the ḥasīb? He said: One who does good deeds with his wealth and without it. This. The conclusion: 'āl' is more specific than 'ahl' absolutely, in that 'ahl' includes rational and non-rational; one says 'ahl of a man' for his property and family, while 'āl' is only for rational beings. Also, among rational beings, 'āl' includes only those with stature, whereas 'ahl' is general. Hence 'āl' is only used for one with worldly or absolute stature, like Āl Firʿawn and Āl Muḥammad (ṣ). It is said: since they performed verbal alteration in 'āl' by changing the hā', they performed the first specification to ensure harmony between word and meaning; and since the hā' is a heavy letter because it is from the deepest part of the throat, when it was turned into alif – a light letter – the word suffered a loss of strength, so they performed the second specification to compensate for this loss. (Muḥammad ʿAlī)

Interpreting Implicit Meaning through Grammar: The Case of 'Jam' Mudaf'
Commentator explains how the phrase implies attainment of highest ranks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the grammatical construction of a construct plural (al-jam' al-mudaf) can imply generality and derive implicit meaning in Islamic commentary.

Core Idea
A construct plural can indicate generality, making the implicit explicit.

In this passage, the commentator defends an interpretation that seems to go beyond the literal wording. He explains that the phrase 'they ascended the ladders of truth' actually implies 'they reached the furthest ranks of truth' because the grammatical form 'maratib al-haqq' (the ranks of truth) is a construct plural, which indicates all ranks. Understanding this grammatical principle is key to appreciating how commentators derive deeper meanings from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Claim and Apparent Gap
The author says 'ascended the ladders of truth' but does not explicitly say 'reached the highest rank'. Why would a commentator think this is implied?
2
Recognize the Grammatical Principle
The term 'maratib al-haqq' is a construct phrase (mudaf mudaf ilayh) where the first word is plural. In Arabic, such a construction (al-jam' al-mudaf) often indicates generality (istighraq) – i.e., all ranks, not just some.
This principle is established in grammar and usul.
3
Apply the Principle to Derive Implication
Since the ascension is of all ranks, ascending all necessarily includes ascending the highest. Therefore, the implicit meaning 'reaching the furthest ranks' is logically entailed.
The commentator argues this entailment is necessary.
4
Check the Reasoning
Is this inference valid? Yes, because if you ascend every rung of a ladder, you must pass through the top rung. The commentator uses this to answer the objection that the wording doesn't bear that interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٨٠) قوله بلغوا اقصى مراتب الحق: قد حقق فى موضعه ان الجمع المضاف يفيد العموم ولذا حمله المحشي على البلوغ باقصى المراتب و علله بان الصعود على جميع المراتب كما يفيده الجمع المضاف يستلزم ذلك اى: الوصول الى اقصى المراتب اى انتهائها و المعارج جمع المعرج و هو المرقاة - الموصلة الى ما هو الحق (عبد الرحيم) (٨١) قوله فان الصعود على جميع مراتبه : استيناف جواب نشأ من الكلام السابق و هو ان الظاهر من كلام المصنف: انهم صعدوا معارج الحق مطلقا ، وليس فيه ما يدل على بلوغهم أقصى مراتب الحق، فالتفسير به تفسير بما لا يتحمله اللفظ . و حاصل الجواب: ان ذلك يستفاد من اضافة الجمع الى المحلى باللام فانه يفيد العموم كما تقرر في محله فيكون المعنى أنهم صعدوا جميع مراتب الحق ولا شك ان الصعود الى جميع المراتب يستلزم الصعود الى اقصى المراتب و الا لايكون الصعود الى جميعها بل الى بعضها كما لا يخفى. (80) His statement: 'they reached the furthest ranks of truth'. It has been established in its place that the construct phrase of a plural (al-jam' al-mudaf) indicates generality. Therefore the commentator interpreted it as reaching the furthest ranks and explained that ascending all ranks, as indicated by the construct plural, necessitates that, i.e., reaching the furthest ranks, i.e., their end. And al-ma'arij is the plural of al-ma'ruj, which is the ladder that leads to what is truth. (Abd al-Rahim). (81) His statement: 'for the ascent upon all its ranks' is a resumptive answer arising from the previous speech, namely that the apparent meaning of the author's speech is that they ascended the ladders of truth absolutely, and there is nothing indicating their reaching the furthest ranks of truth; so interpreting it as such is an interpretation that the wording does not bear. The gist of the answer is that this is understood from the addition of the plural to the definite article, for it indicates generality, as established in its place. So the meaning is that they ascended all the ranks of truth, and there is no doubt that ascending all ranks entails ascending the furthest ranks; otherwise it would not be ascending all but only some, as is not hidden. (Mirza Muhammad Ali). (82) His statement: 'by investigation' is connected to 'they ascended', and the ba is for causality, as in his previous statement 'by assent'. The meaning: they ascended the ladders of truth and reached the furthest truth due to investigation and certainty. It may also indicate the reading: this judgment is certain, no doubt in it. (Jalal al-Din al-Dawani). (83) i.e., like what passed: meaning the author's statement 'by assent', i.e., just as it is a laghw zarf connected to 'they were blessed', so too his statement 'by investigation' is a laghw zarf connected to 'they ascended'. (Muhammad Ali).

Commentary
This passage explains the interpretation of the author's phrase 'they reached the furthest ranks of truth'. The commentator defends the interpretation that the text implies reaching the highest rank, even though the literal wording only says 'they ascended the ladders of truth'. The argument relies on the grammatical principle that a construct phrase of a plural (al-jam' al-mudaf) like 'maratib al-haqq' indicates generality, meaning they ascended all ranks. Ascending all ranks necessarily entails reaching the highest rank. Additional remarks clarify the grammatical connection of 'by investigation' and compare it to 'by assent' in a previous phrase.

Deletion Rules for the General Verb Factor
Author explains when deletion of the muta'allaq is obligatory and when it is not, with examples and citations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four syntactic positions where deletion of the muta'allaq is obligatory and distinguish between general and specific verbs in Arabic grammar.

Core Idea
Deletion of the general verb factor is obligatory in four positions: predicate, relative clause, adjective, circumstantial.

In this passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya, we continue examining the muta'allaq (attached element) for adverbs (zarf). The author specifies when the muta'allaq must be deleted and when it must be mentioned, citing earlier grammarians. This rule is essential for correctly parsing Arabic syntax, especially in Quranic verses and classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
Find the four syntactic contexts listed: khabar (predicate), silah (relative clause), sifah (adjective), and hal (circumstantial accusative). Understand that in these positions, the general verb factor is obligatorily deleted.
The zarf itself suffices to fill the grammatical role.
2
Examine the disagreement
Note that Ibn Jinni permitted mentioning the verb, but al-Radi refuted this claim, stating there is no evidence. Recognize that the authoritative view is obligatory deletion.
3
Analyze the exceptions
Review the Quranic verse (Q. 27:40), Imam Ali's sermon, and the poetic line. Determine that in these cases, the verb is specific (not general), so deletion is not obligatory.
The verb 'ka'in' can mean 'residing' or 'stationary', not a general existential verb.
4
Contrast with specific verbs
Understand that for specific verbs, the factor must be pronounced unless contextual evidence supports deletion. The example 'I prayed in the mosque' vs 'Zayd is in the bath' illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أعلم انه اذا كان المتعلق من الافعال العامة يحذف وجوباً في المواضع الأربعة اعني: الخبر والصلة والصفة والحال لقيام القرينة على تعيينه و صد الظرف .مسده و قال ابن جنى بجوازه. قال الرضى ولا شاهد له. و اما قوله تعالى: (فلما رأه مستقراً عنده فمعناه ساكناً غير متحرك وليس بمعنى كائناً ، انتهى . و اما ما وقع فى بعض خطب امير المؤمنين عليه الصلوة والسلام في وصفه تعالى من قوله (ع): «لم يحل فى الاشياء فيقال هو فيها كائن...» فقال بعض المحققين كان الكائن فيه من الكون في لاشياء بمعنى الحلول فليس من الامور العامة حتى يجب حذفه. و اما قوله لك العزّ ان مولاك عزوان يهن فانت لدى بحبوة الهون كائن فضرورة أو بمعنى ساكن وفيا عدا المواضع الاربعة لا يتعلق الظرف و الجار الا بملفوظ ،موجود، صرح بذلك الرضى (ره) و اما اذا كان من الافعال الخاصة فلا يحذف الا لدليل كما تقول انى صليت في المسجد وزيد في الحمام فصلوة اينا افضل؟ فاحفظ هذه الجملة.
Then know that when the attached element is from the general verbs, it is obligatorily deleted in the four positions: namely, predicate (khabar), relative clause (silah), adjective (sifah), and circumstantial accusative (hal), because there is a contextual indicator specifying it and the zarf takes its place. Ibn Jinni permitted its mention. Al-Radi said he has no evidence. As for His saying, Exalted: 'When he saw it settled with him'—its meaning is stationary, not moving, not meaning 'existent'. End. As for what occurs in some sermons of the Commander of the Faithful (peace be upon him) describing the Exalted, his saying: 'He does not reside in things such that it is said He is in them...' Some verifying scholars said that 'ka'in' here is from kawn in the sense of 'residing' (hulul), not from the general matters so as to require its deletion. As for the saying: 'To you is glory because your patron is generous, so you are in the expanse of humiliation, existing'—it is a poetic necessity or it means 'stationary'. And in other than the four positions, the zarf and the prepositional phrase are only attached to a pronounced, existing [factor], as clearly stated by al-Radi. And when the [attached element] is from the specific verbs, it is not deleted except with evidence, as when you say: 'I prayed in the mosque, and Zayd is in the bath, which prayer is better?' So memorize this sentence.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of zarf mustaqir by examining the deletion rules for the muta'allaq (attached element) when it is a general verb. The author states that deletion is obligatory in four syntactic positions: khabar (predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is in the house'), silah (relative clause of an ism mawṣul), sifah (adjective describing an indefinite noun), and hal (circumstantial accusative). The reason is that the context provides a clear indicator of the intended factor, and the zarf itself suffices to fill the position. Two grammarians are cited: Ibn Jinni permits mentioning the verb (making deletion optional), but al-Radi asserts there is no attested evidence for Ibn Jinni's view. The author then examines several exceptions: (a) The Quranic verse 'When he saw it settled with him' (Q. 27:40): here 'mustaqirran' means 'stationary', not a general verb, so it is not a case of mustaqir zarf. (b) In a sermon of Imam Ali, 'ka'in' is interpreted as 'residing' (hulul), a specific verb, not a general one. (c) A line of poetry uses 'ka'in' under poetic necessity or meaning 'stationary'. Thus, outside the four obligatory positions, the factor must be explicitly pronounced. When the factor is a specific verb, deletion is not allowed without contextual evidence. The author gives an example contrasting 'I prayed in the mosque' (where the verb 'prayed' is specific and must be mentioned) with 'Zayd is in the bath' (where only context distinguishes the meanings). This lesson reinforces the grammatical rules governing the use of adverbs and their deletion.

Key Terms
المواضع الأربعة
القرينة
المتعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, deletion of the muta'allaq is obligatory in all of the following EXCEPT?
A) Predicate (khabar)
B) Subject (fa'il)
C) Relative clause (silah)
D) Circumstantial accusative (hal)

Key Takeaway
The muta'allaq of general verbs must be deleted in four specific syntactic contexts; otherwise it must be pronounced unless contextual evidence allows deletion for specific verbs.

Terminological Sensitivity: From 'Laghw' to 'Khass'
Author cites a scholar's preference to replace 'laghw' with 'khass' due to its Quranic/hadith occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify terminological shifts in logical texts motivated by reverence for sacred language and explain the underlying logical rationale.

Core Idea
Technical terms can be changed out of respect for Quranic usage without altering the underlying concept.

In Islamic scholarship, terminology is not arbitrary; it is often influenced by the gravity of sacred texts. This passage shows how a scholar avoided the term 'laghw' because it appears in the Quran and hadith, preferring 'khass' to preserve reverence. The author analogizes to al-Sakkaki's preference for 'fasila' over 'saj' for Quranic rhyme. Understanding this helps us see how adab (propriety) shapes technical language in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target term and its replacement
Find the term 'laghw' in the Arabic passage and note that it is replaced by 'khass'. Also 'mustaqir' is replaced by 'amm'.
The replacement is not arbitrary but based on the specific vs. general nature of the factor.
2
Understand the motive for the shift
The scholar says he dislikes calling the zarf 'laghw' because the word 'laghw' occurs in the Quran and hadith (e.g., 'laghw' meaning vain talk). Using it as a grammatical term might show disrespect.
This motive is rooted in reverence for the sacred text.
3
Note the logical basis of the distinction
The passage explains that in 'khass' (formerly 'laghw'), the factor is specific; in 'amm' (formerly 'mustaqir'), it is general. The logical concept remains unchanged.
The shift is only terminological; the underlying logical division is preserved.
4
Apply the analogy to al-Sakkaki's preference
Al-Sakkaki disliked calling Quranic rhyme 'saj' because 'saj' was used by pre-Islamic soothsayers. He replaced it with 'fasila'. Similarly, 'ru'us' (heads) is used for verse endings.
This shows a pattern of terminological refinement across Islamic disciplines.
5
Reflect on the principle of adab in technical language
Consider how this awareness of sacred language influences other fields like fiqh or kalam, where terms like 'hukm' or 'amr' are used with care.
Terminological choices are not neutral; they reflect respect for divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال بعضهم: انى لا احب تسمية الظرف باللغو لوقوعه في التنزيل و الحديث، فلا يخلو عن سوء ادب بل اسمى اللغوخاصاً والمستقر عاماً اذ الملحوظ في الاول خصوص العامل و في الثاني عمومه و هذا نظير ما قال السكاكي: لا احب ان اسعى السجع سجعاً لوقوعه في كلام الحكيم تعالى بل اسميه فاصلة و لذا ايضاً تريهم يعبرون عن اواخر الآى بالرؤس، فافهم . (میرزا محمد علی)
Then some said: I do not like calling the zarf 'laghw' because it occurs in the Revelation and hadith, so it is not free from poor manners. Rather I call 'laghw' as 'specific' (khāṣṣ) and 'mustaqir' as 'general' ('āmm), since what is observed in the first is the specificity of the factor and in the second its generality. And this is analogous to what al-Sakkaki said: I do not like to call saj' 'saj'' because it occurs in the speech of the Wise, Exalted, but rather I call it fasila. For this reason too, you see them refer to the ends of verses as 'heads'. So understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This short passage addresses a terminological sensitivity. Some scholars object to using the term 'laghw' (trivial) for a type of zarf because the word 'laghw' appears in the Quran and hadith with meanings like 'vain talk' or 'triviality', and applying it to a grammatical term might imply disrespect. Therefore, they prefer to replace 'laghw' with 'khass' (specific) and 'mustaqir' with 'amm' (general), since the distinction actually rests on whether the factor is specific or general. The author draws an analogy with al-Sakkaki, who disliked calling the rhymed prose of the Quran 'saj' (like pre-Islamic soothsayers), preferring the term 'fasila' (pause). Similarly, scholars refer to verse endings as 'ru'us' (heads) to avoid the term 'saj'. The lesson teaches that technical terminology can be adjusted out of reverence for sacred texts, but the underlying concepts remain unchanged.

الفاصلة

Lexical Explanation of 'Nisyan Mansiyyan'
Author explains the phrase 'nisyan mansiyyan' from the matn, citing Quranic interpretation and lexical nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'nisyan mansiyyan' as used in the matn, including its lexical nuance and the commentator's analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
The phrase 'nisyan mansiyyan' means something so insignificant it deserves to be discarded, not merely forgotten.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the phrase 'nisyan mansiyyan' from the matn. He clarifies its pronunciation, cites a Quranic verse, and gives a striking analogy to ensure you grasp the precise connotation. Understanding this nuance is key to recognizing when a deleted element in idafa is intended to be ignored.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its pronunciation
Note the phrase 'nisyan mansiyyan' from the matn. The commentator points out that 'al-nisy' can be pronounced with kasr or fatha on the nun. This variant affects the reading but not the core meaning.
2
Analyze the Quranic citation
The commentator cites the verse 'wa kuntum nisyan mansiyyan' (Q. 19:23). Reflect on the context of Maryam giving birth; the phrase suggests being forgotten and abandoned. This Quranic usage grounds the lexical meaning in revelation.
The verse is from Maryam's story, where she wishes she had been forgotten.
3
Unpack the analogical reasoning
The commentator compares 'nisy' to a menstrual rag (khirqat al-ha'id) — something ritually impure and deserving to be thrown away and forgotten. He also draws a parallel with 'dhabh' (slaughter), where the word itself names what is fit to be slaughtered. This analogy shows that 'nisy' is not merely forgotten but inherently worthy of being discarded.
The analogy emphasizes the inherent worthlessness, not just the fact of being forgotten.
4
Distinguish from 'manwiyyan'
The commentator contrasts 'nisyan mansiyyan' with 'manwiyyan', which means something attended to in the mind. This contrast clarifies that the deleted element in idafa is either completely ignored (like 'nisyan mansiyyan') or mentally present (like 'manwiyyan').
This distinction is crucial for understanding the types of idafa deletion in logic.

When Are Ghayat Adverbs Built? The Third State Explained
Author explains the third state of ghayat adverbs being built due to intended deletion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three states of ghayat adverbs and determine when they are built (mabniyya) based on the presence or intention of the madaf ilayhi.

Core Idea
An adverb is built when its deleted complement is still intended; intention creates grammatical dependency.

In Arabic grammar, ghayat adverbs like qabl and ba'd can be either declinable (mu'rab) or invariable (mabni) depending on the condition of their madaf ilayhi (the noun they are added to). The matn states "wa 'ala al-thalith mabniyya" (and on the third, built). The hashya by Mirza Muhammad Ali unpacks this third state, distinguishing it from the other two by the critical factor of intention. This lesson will guide you through the three states, focusing on the third, which is the most subtle.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three states of ghayat adverbs
The first state: the madaf ilayhi is present and overt (e.g., qabla al-zuhr). The second state: the madaf ilayhi is deleted and completely forgotten (laysa manwiyyan). The third state: the madaf ilayhi is deleted but intended (manwiyy).
Memorize these three states.
2
Focus on the third state's condition: intention (al-niyya)
In the third state, the speaker has a specific reference in mind but omits it. For example, 'kuntu qablan' means 'I was formerly' without stating relative to what, but the context implies a known event.
The term 'manwiyy' is key.
3
Understand why need triggers building
The adverb needs the madaf ilayhi for completion. When the madaf ilayhi is deleted but still intended, that need remains active, just like particles (huruf) that require an implicit complement. This need causes the adverb to become built (mabni).
Contrast this with the second state where there is no need, so the adverb stays mu'rab.
4
Contrast with the first and second states
First state: madaf ilayhi present → idafa is explicit, favoring i'rab (nominality). Second state: deleted and unintended → no need, so adverb remains declinable. Third state: deleted but intended → need persists, so adverb is built.
Use the table in your mind to compare.
5
Apply the rule to new examples
Given a sentence like 'jā'a ba'dun' (he came later), determine the state. Since 'ba'd' is built (damma), and there is no explicit madaf ilayhi, it must be in the third state (if a reference is intended) or second state (if not). Context decides.
Practice with multiple sentences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ذلك (۸۹) قوله و على الثالث مبنية : اى على تقدير ان لا يذكر معها المضاف اليه وكان منوياً. المشابهتها الحرف في الاحتياج الى ذلك المحذوف بخلاف الحالة الثانية فانها لما كان المحذوف فيها نسياً منسياً غير مراد للمتكلم بها لم يكن محتاجاً اليه لتكون هذه الظروف مبنية للاحتياج اليه فمعنى كنت قبلاً اى قديماً وكنت بعداً اى آخراً و هكذا من غير ملاحظة شيء يعتبر التقدم والتأخر بالنسبة اليه و بخلاف الحالة الاولى فانه وان كان الاحتياج حاصلاً لها مع وجود المضاف اليه ايضاً لكن ظهور الاضافة فيها يرجح جانب الاسمية لاختصاصها بالاسماء. (میرزا محمد علی)
And that: (89) His saying: 'and on the third, built': i.e. on the assumption that the madaf ilayhi is not mentioned with it and is intended. They resemble the harf (particle) in needing that deleted [element], in contrast to the second state, for since the deleted [element] in it is nisyan mansiyyan, not intended by the speaker, they do not need it; so these adverbs are built due to the need for it. Thus, the meaning of 'kuntu qablan' is 'I was formerly', and 'kuntu ba'dan' is 'I was later', and so on, without observing anything regarding which precedence and posterity are considered. And in contrast to the first state, for even though the need is present for them with the existence of the madaf ilayhi as well, the appearance of idafa in them favors the side of nominality (ismiyya) because it is specific to nouns. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage clarifies the third state (case) of ghayat adverbs, where they are built (mabniyya). The matn's phrase is 'wa 'ala al-thalith mabniyya' (and on the third, built). The hashya explains that this occurs when the madaf ilayhi (the noun to which the adverb is added) is deleted but intended (manwiyy) in the speaker's mind. In this state, the adverbs resemble particles (harf) because they need that deleted element for their meaning. The author contrasts this with the second state, where the deleted element is 'nisyan mansiyyan' (forgotten and discarded) and not intended, so the adverbs do not need it and are not built (they remain declinable? Actually, the text says they are built due to need, but in the second state they are not needed, so perhaps they are not built? The passage is ambiguous; it says 'these adverbs are built due to the need for it' meaning in the third state. In the second state, since there is no need, they are not built? The commentator clarifies that in the first state (where madaf ilayhi is present), the idafa is explicit and favors nominality (i'rab), so the adverb is not built. So the third state is unique: the madaf ilayhi is deleted but intended, creating a grammatical need that triggers building, analogous to particles that require an implicit complement. This lesson refines the understanding of when these adverbs are invariable.

D) Because they are nouns

Key Takeaway
The third state of ghayat adverbs is defined by the deleted madaf ilayhi being intended, which creates a particle-like need for it, resulting in the adverb being built.

The Fa' in 'Fahadha': Two Grammatical Explanations
Author explains the fa' in 'fahadha' as either due to an imagined preceding 'amma' or an omitted 'amma' in the speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain two possible origins of the fa' in 'fahadha' as either an imagined 'amma' construction or an estimated 'amma' in the speech.

Core Idea
The fa' in 'fahadha' implies an underlying conditional 'amma...fa' structure.

In Arabic logical and rhetorical texts, the particle fa' often signals a response to a preceding condition. In this commentary on the phrase 'fahadha', the author unpacks the grammatical assumptions behind the fa'. Understanding this helps you better interpret connector words in Islamic scholarly works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'fahadha' in the matn
Locate the phrase and note that it begins with fa'. This fa' is the subject of analysis.
2
Recognize the two possibilities given by the author
The author states that the fa' comes either from imagining a preceding 'amma' (with alif omitted) or from estimating an 'amma' in the speech even if deleted.
3
Understand the 'amma...fa' structure
Recall that 'amma' is a conditional particle used in phrases like 'amma ba'd fa...'. The fa' is the response to that condition, justifying its presence here.
4
Consider why this matters for reading
Correctly understanding the fa' affects translation and interpretation of the logical flow in classical texts, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹٠) قوله فهذا هذا الفاء الداخلة على اسم الاشارة اما ان يكون اتيانها على توهم المتكلم أو : الكاتب انه جاء قبلها بكلمة اما واما يؤتى بعدها بالغاء أو على تقدير اما في نظم الكلام و ان كانت محذوفة
(90) His saying: 'fahadha'—this fa' entering on the demonstrative noun may either be its coming based on the speaker's or writer's assumption that before it came the word 'amma' and after it 'alif' is omitted, or on the estimation of 'amma' in the organization of the speech even if it is deleted.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage analyzes the particle 'fa' in the phrase 'fahadha' (so this). The author gives two possible explanations for its occurrence: either it is used because the speaker imagines that a preceding clause like 'amma' exists (as in 'amma ba'd, fa-hadha...'), with the 'alif' of 'amma' omitted in writing, or it is based on an underlying structure where 'amma' is estimated in the speech even if it is not expressed. The lesson thus addresses a minute point of Arabic syntax within the commentary tradition, showing how logical connectors like 'fa' can be explained by ellipsis or assumption of a conditional structure.

Key Terms

Seven Possible Referents of 'This' and Their Elimination
Author lists and discusses seven possible references of the word 'this' to the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven possible referents of the word 'this' when referring to a book and apply the principles of discourse definition and homonymy to eliminate invalid referents, leaving only words and meanings.

Core Idea
Only words and meanings are valid referents because inscriptions are not discourse and composites violate the rule of homonymy.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a brief remark by the original author, who limited the referent of 'this' to two possibilities. The commentator identifies seven logical possibilities based on the various meanings of 'book', then systematically eliminates five using two key principles: the definition of discourse and the rule against using a homonymous word for multiple referents. By following this reasoning, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate definitions and referential claims in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the seven possibilities
List all seven possible referents derived from the meanings of 'book': words, meanings, inscriptions, and composites of any two or all three.
2
Identify the definition of discourse
Recall that discourse (kalām) is defined as either verbal or mental. This excludes anything that is not spoken or thought.
3
Eliminate inscriptions
Since inscriptions are neither verbal nor mental, they are not discourse, so they cannot be the referent.
4
Eliminate composites involving inscriptions
A composite of a thing and something else is not identical to the thing itself; thus composites that include inscriptions are not the book.
5
Apply the rule of homonymy
The composite of words and meanings would require 'this' to refer to both simultaneously, but a homonymous word cannot be used for two different referents at once. Eliminate that too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانما اقتصر على هذين الاحتمالين مع انه اشارة الى الكتاب و هو كما يطلق على الالفاظ والمعانى كذلك يطلق على النقوش والمركب من الاثنين والثلاثة فتكون هينا سبعة احتمالات اذ لا يجوز حينئذ حمل غاية تهذيب الكلام عليه لان الكلام اما لفظی او معنوی فاحتمال كون المشار اليه هو النقوش ليس بجائز لانها ليست بكلام وكذا المركب من الاثنين والثلاثة اذالمركب من الشيء وغيره غيره كما فى غير المعانى والالفاظ و اما فيهما فلعدم صحة اطلاق لفظ المشترك على المعنيين فلابد ان يكون اشارة الى الالفاظ او المعانى
He restricted himself to these two possibilities, even though it (the word 'this') is a reference to the book. And just as it is applied to words and meanings, similarly it is applied to inscriptions and to the composite of two or three. Thus there would be seven possibilities. But then it is not permissible to attribute 'The Ultimate Aim of the Discipline of Discourse' to it, because discourse (al-kalām) is either verbal or mental. So the possibility that the referent is inscriptions is not permissible, because they are not discourse. Similarly for the composite of two or three, because the composite of a thing and other than it is other than it, as in the case of non-meanings and non-words. As for the composite of meanings and words, that would require using a homonymous word (lafẓ mushtarak) in more than one sense, which is not permissible. Therefore it must be a reference to words or to meanings.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage discusses the possible referents of the word 'this' when it is said to refer to 'the book'. The author notes that 'book' can apply to words, meanings, inscriptions, or composites of two or three of these, yielding seven possibilities. However, only two are acceptable: reference to words or to meanings. Inscriptions are not discourse (kalām), which is either verbal or mental. Composites involving inscriptions or other combinations also fail because a composite of a thing and something else is not identical to that thing. Furthermore, using a homonymous term for both meanings and words would violate the rule against multiple simultaneous senses. Hence only the two original possibilities remain valid.

Key Terms
الكلام
اللفظ المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The referent of a word like 'this' when referring to a book can only be words or meanings because inscriptions are not discourse and composites violate the rule against homonymy.

The word 'sawā' with 'aw' vs 'am'
Author defends use of 'aw' after 'sawā' by citing its well-known meaning as 'wāw'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve apparent contradictions between disjunction and equality in Arabic logical statements by recognizing when 'aw' functions as 'wāw'.

Core Idea
Equality requires two; 'aw' selects one, but Arabic usage permits 'aw' to mean 'and'.

The conjunction 'aw' (or) usually indicates a choice between alternatives, but when combined with 'sawā' (equal), a conflict arises: equality needs two items, while 'aw' singles out one. In this lesson, we analyze how classical scholars resolved this by appealing to a well-known Arabic usage where 'aw' carries the sense of 'wāw' (and). This grammatical nuance is essential for correctly interpreting logical and legal statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that 'aw' is incorrect after 'sawā' because 'aw' denotes one of multiple things, whereas equality requires multiple things. Locate this objection in the Arabic text.
The objection begins with 'فان قلت'.
2
Extract the author's response
The author responds by stating that 'aw' is used in the sense of 'wāw' in well-known Arabic. Find the exact wording of the response and note the evidence he provides.
The response starts with 'قلت: استعمال (أو بمعنى الواو مشهور'.
3
Analyze the underlying logical principle
Consider the logical difference: 'aw' normally indicates exclusive disjunction, while equality requires inclusion of all alternatives. The author's solution reinterprets 'aw' as inclusive conjunction. Reflect on how this affects the meaning of the statement.
The same issue applies to 'am' as discussed later.
4
Apply to the Qur'anic example
The author cites the verse 'سواء عليهم أنذرتهم أم لم تنذرهم' (Q 2:6). Explain why this usage is acceptable even though 'am' also seems to select one. Use the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) to harmonize grammar and logic.
The verse uses 'am', which the author says is also problematic under a strict analysis, but the consensus accepts it.

Source Text

قوله سواء. السواء اسم مصدر بمعنى الاستواء يوصف به كما يوصف بالمصادر و منه قوله تعالى: «تعالوا الى كلمة سواء بيننا وبينكم و ما بعده في تأويل المصدر مبتداء هو خبر والتقدير وكونها قبل التصنيف أو بعده سواء. فان قلت: كلمة «او» لاحد المتعدد والتسوية انما يكون بين المتعدد لا بين احده فالصواب الواو بدل «او» في قول المحشى: «... قبل التصنيف أو بعده»). قلت: استعمال (أو بمعنى الواو مشهور نعم يرد ذلك اذا استعمل سواء بكلمة ام المتصلة كما في قوله تعالى: «سواء عليهم أنذرتهم ام لم تنذرهم ... وتمام الكلام مذكور في حواشي المطول . ثم لا يذهب عليك ان ما ذكره ابوعلى يقتضى ان لا يجوز العطف بام ايضاً فانها ايضاً لاحد القسمين الشيئين او الاشياء فلا يجوز سواء على اقمت ام قعدت لان المعنى سواء على احدهما وما ذلك الا لجعلهم سواء خبرا و ما بعده مبتداء، فالحق احق بان يتبع والصدق حقيق بان يستمع.
His statement 'sawā'. 'Sawā' is a verbal noun meaning equality; it is used as an adjective like verbal nouns. From it is His statement: 'Come to a word that is equal between us and you' (Q 3:64). What follows is interpreted as a verbal noun, subject, and it is predicate. The estimation: 'and its being before composition or after it is equal.' If you say: The word 'or' (aw) is for one of multiple, while equality is between multiple, not between one of them, so the correct thing is 'and' (wāw) instead of 'or' in the marginalist's statement: '...before composition or after it.' I say: The use of 'aw' in the meaning of 'wāw' is well-known. Yes, that is objected to when 'sawā' is used with the connected 'am', as in His statement: 'It is equal to them whether you warn them or do not warn them' (Q 2:6). And the complete discussion is mentioned in the glosses of al-Muṭawwal. Then let it not escape you that what Abū 'Alī mentioned requires that conjunction with 'am' is also not permissible, for it too is for one of two divisions of things, so 'It is equal to me whether you stood or sat' is not permissible, because the meaning would be 'It is equal to me one of them.' And that is only because they make 'sawā' predicate and what follows subject. So truth is more worthy of being followed, and honesty is deserving of being heard.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and logical discussion of the word 'sawā' (equal) and its permissible particles. The author reports an objection: 'aw' indicates one of multiple, but equality requires at least two, so the correct conjunction should be 'wāw' (and) not 'aw' (or). The response is that 'aw' can be used in the sense of 'wāw', which is a known usage. However, if one adopts the analysis of Abū 'Alī, even 'am' would be problematic because it also singles out one of two. The author sides with the view that 'sawā' can be followed by either 'aw' or 'am' depending on syntactic analysis, and concludes that the truth should be followed.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive particle 'aw' can function as the conjunctive 'wāw' in Arabic, enabling its use after equality expressions like 'sawā'.

Refuting external reference for 'this' in prefaces
Author critiques and refines the view that 'this' refers to external inscriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of the view that 'this' refers to external inscriptions and reconstruct the argument that it must refer to a mental universal.

Core Idea
External things are particular; 'this' in a preface refers to a universal, so reference is mental.

In this passage, the author critically examines the claim that 'this' in a preface points to external inscriptions on the page. At first glance, this might seem plausible, but the author shows it leads to contradictions. You will learn how the distinction between particular and universal refutes this view and establishes that the referent is always mental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target view
Find the claim being criticized: that when the preface is composed after the main text, the word 'this' refers to something externally present (like written inscriptions).
2
Extract the author's objection
The author argues that external presence is only possible for particular (shakhṣī) entities, but the word 'this' in such contexts refers to a universal (kullī) concept, which has no external existence as a universal.
The author earlier noted that words and meanings have no external existence.
3
Analyze the metaphorical proposal
Consider the suggestion that 'this' is used metaphorically (naming the expressed by the expression). The author finds this problematic because it would involve describing a particular with a name that signifies a universal.
4
Conclude the mental reference
Since the referent cannot be an external particular or a metaphorical particular, the only consistent interpretation is that 'this' refers to a mental entity—a universal concept present in the mind.

His statement: 'Since there is no existence of words nor of meanings in the external.' So what is said—that if the preface is placed after the composition, then the reference is to what is present externally—is not sound unless by it is meant reference to the inscriptions of writing, without their meanings, and without the composite of two or three of them. And it is not hidden that this does not suit this context because it is described as 'The Ultimate Aim of the Discipline of Discourse', unless it is taken metaphorically, naming the expressed by the name of the expression. But there is consideration in that after what is not hidden from the intelligent. For what is present can only be particular, and it is clear that what is meant is not describing that particular, nor naming that particular with that name, flowing from the description of its type. Its naming is the written inscriptions that signify those specific words placed in correspondence to specific meanings, whether that particular or other than it shares in that concept. And there is no doubt that there is no external presence for this universal. Thus the reference is to what is present in the mind in all cases.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous view. The author argues that even when the preface is written after composition, the referent of 'this' is not external inscriptions but mental entities. He points out that what is present externally is a particular (shakhṣī), but the word 'this' in such contexts refers to a universal concept (kullī) that is not present externally. Therefore, the reference is always to something present in the mind. The suggestion that it could be metaphorical is considered but found lacking. Hence, the only consistent interpretation is that the reference is to mental content in all situations.

Interpreting Metaphorical Expressions: Ghaya Tahdhib al-Kalam
Commentary on interpreting the book's description in the preface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a literal interpretation is impossible and apply the concepts of hyperbole (mubalagha) and figurative deletion (majaz al-hadhf) to interpret problematic statements in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A book cannot literally be a concept; read it as hyperbole or assume a deletion.

In the preface, the author describes his book as 'the utmost refinement of speech'. But a book is a physical object, not a quality. The commentator resolves this by proposing several interpretive strategies: hyperbole, where the object is named by its attribute for emphasis, or deletion, where words have been omitted. This lesson explores these strategies and their application in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal problem
The phrase 'this book is the utmost refinement' is literally false because a book is an object, not a quality. Recognize that the meaning cannot be carried on the entity (العين).
The commentator explicitly notes that 'the meaning is not borne by the entity'.
2
Recognize the need for interpretation
Since the literal meaning is corrupt (fasid), the commentator looks for alternative readings: either the phrase is hyperbolic or there is a deletion.
This step is crucial in logical analysis: when a statement is impossible, reinterpret it within acceptable rhetorical conventions.
3
Explore the hyperbole solution
The book itself is called 'refinement' as a metaphor, similar to saying 'Zaid is justice'. The verbal noun (مصدر) is taken as hyperbole, not as a literal predicate.
Hyperbole (مبالغة) is a common figure of speech in Arabic, allowing the thing itself to stand for its attribute for emphasis.
4
Explore the deletion solution
Assume that words have been omitted. For example, the original might be 'this book is purified (مهذب) with a purification that is the utmost refinement', where the agent is deleted and the absolute object replaces it. Other possibilities include deleting the مضاف (added part) or making the verbal noun a passive participle.
This is called مجاز الحذف (figurative deletion), where the deletion of a word transfers the meaning to another.
5
Check for consistency with the work's content
The commentator anticipates an objection: if refinement means removing superfluities, does this book contain superfluities? He leaves the answer for later, showing that the interpretive process is ongoing and critical.
This step demonstrates that interpretation must be consistent with the actual content of the work.

(۹۸) قوله حمله على هذا الغرض من هذا الكلام اصلاح كلام المصنف فان ظاهره فاسد لظهور ان المعنى لا يحمل على العين كما سبق فى شرح قول المصنف : ارسله» هدى هو بالاهتداء حقيق. ثم انما اختصر هنا على كون الحمل للمبالغة او تقدير الخبر و لم يجوز كون المصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول كما جوز فيما سبق كونه بمعنى اسم الفاعل لان الاضافة الى الكلام لا يلائم ذلك كما هو ظاهر. اللهم الا ان يكون من قبيل اضافة الصفة الى الموصوف فتأمل. وقد عرفت سابقاً وجهاً آخر لحل الاشكال في امثال ذلك فراجعه . (محمد علی) (۹۹) قوله أما على المبالغة لما كان كلام المصنف غير مرتبط بحسب الظاهر اذلايصح ان يقال : ان الكتاب غاية تهذيب اى غاية تنقيح فان المعنى لا يحمل على العين بحسب الحقيقة، فاجاب المحشى بان هذا الحمل اما بطريق المجاز حيث جعل العين نفس المعنى للمبالغة كما في نحو «زيد عدل» فلم يحتمل الخبر الضمير. قال الكوفيون: المصدر يؤل بالمشتق دائماً فيكون الحمل بحسب الحقيقة والجنسية متحملاً للضمير او بناء على ان التقدير هذا الكتاب مهذب تحذف كثيراً ما عامل المفعول المطلق و اقيم المفعول المطلق مقامه و يحتمل ان يكون التقدير: هذا الكتاب مهذب تهذيباً غاية التهذيب فحذف العامل واقيم المفعول المطلق مقامه ثم حذف المفعول المطلق و اقيم صفته مقامه او يكون تصنيف هذا الكتاب غاية تهذيب الكلام بحذف المضاف أو يكون هذا الكتاب غاية كلام مهذب بان يجعل المصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول والاضافة من قبيل جرد قطيفة. ولقائل ان يقول ان تهذيب الكلام تنقيحه وتطهيره من المعايب و الزوائد، فكيف يوصف به هذا الكتاب مع اشتماله على بعض الزوائد ؟ كما سيجيء الاشارة الى بعضها . (عبد الرحيم) (۱۰۰) الاظهر انه على صيغة اسم المفعول ويجوز على بعد ان يقرء على صيغة الفاعل ايضاً. (محمد على) (۱۰۱) فصار تقدير الكلام هذا الكلام غاية التهذيب فحذف اللام وعوضت عنها الاضافة لتعيين المقصود و لرعاية السجع ثم استغنى عن وصف اسم الاشارة لدلالة الكلام عليه . ( محمد علی) (۱۰۲) قوله على طريقة مجاز الحذف قال المصنف فى شرح التلخيص: اعلم ان الكلمة كما توصف بالمجاز لنقلها عن معناها الاصلى كذلك توصف به ايضاً لنقلها عن اعرابها الاصلى الى غيره بحذف لفظ او زيادة لفظ فالاول كقوله تعالى: «و جاء ربك»، واسئل القرية» والثاني مثل قوله تعالى: لیس كمثله شیء» اى جاء امر ربك لاستحالة مجيء الرب واسئل اهل القرية للمقطع بان المقصود سؤال أهل القرية و ان كان الله تعالى قادراً على انطاق الجدران و ليس مثله شيء لان المقصود نفى ان يكون شيء مثله تعالى لا نفى ان يكون شيء مثل مثله» انتهى ملخصاً. و ما نحن فيه من القسم الاول والدليل على الحذف عدم صحة الحمل بدونه. (98) His statement: carrying it to this purpose — this statement is a correction of the author's phrase, for its apparent meaning is corrupt, because it is clear that the meaning is not borne by the entity (eye), as previously in explaining the author's statement 'arsalahu' 'huda' is truly by guidance. Then he abbreviated here by making the carrying for hyperbole or estimation of the predicate, and he did not allow the verbal noun to be in the sense of the passive participle, as he allowed previously it to be in the sense of the active participle, because the addition to 'speech' does not suit that, as is apparent. Unless it is of the type of attributing the adjective to the described, so reflect. You have already known another way to solve the problem in such cases, so refer to it. (Muhammad Ali) (99) His statement: "as for hyperbole" — since the author's statement is apparently disconnected, because it is not correct to say: 'this book is the utmost refinement', i.e. utmost purification, because the meaning is not carried on the entity (eye) in reality. So the commentator answered that this carrying is either via metaphor, where he made the entity itself the meaning for hyperbole, as in 'Zaid [is] justice', so the predicate does not require a pronoun. The Kufans said: the verbal noun is always interpreted as a derived word, so the carrying is according to reality, and the noun of genus carries the pronoun. Or based on the estimation: 'this book is purified (muhadhdhab)' — often the agent of the absolute object is deleted and the absolute object takes its place. And it is possible that the estimation is: 'this book is purified with a purification that is the utmost purification'; then the agent is deleted and the absolute object takes its place, then the absolute object is deleted and its adjective takes its place. Or: 'the composition of this book is the utmost refinement of speech' by deletion of the added part. Or: 'this book is the utmost refined speech' by making the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle, and the addition is of the type of 'jurd qatifa' (a type of possessive). And one may say: the refinement of speech is its purification and cleansing from defects and superfluities; how can this book be described as such when it contains some superfluities? As will be indicated later. (Abd al-Rahim) (100) The most apparent is that it is on the pattern of the passive participle, though it may be read on the pattern of the active participle as well. (Muhammad Ali) (101) So the estimation of the statement becomes 'this speech is the utmost refinement'; then the lam is deleted and replaced by idafa to specify the intended and to preserve the rhyme; then there is no need for describing the demonstrative noun because the speech indicates it. (Muhammad Ali) (102) His statement: "on the method of figurative deletion" — the author said in Sharh al-Talkhis: know that a word is described as figurative for its transfer from its original meaning, and also for its transfer from its original inflection to another by deleting a word or adding a word. The first is like His statement: 'And your Lord came' and 'Ask the village'; the second is like His statement: 'Nothing is like Him', i.e. the command of your Lord came — because it is impossible for the Lord to come; and ask the people of the village because the intent is to ask the people of the village, even though God is capable of making the walls speak; and 'nothing is like Him' because the intent is to deny that anything is like Him, not to deny that anything is like His like. End of summary. And our case is of the first type, and the evidence for deletion is that the meaning is not correct without it. (Muhammad Ali) (103) For 'tahrir' is the expression free from verbiage and superfluities, unlike 'bayan', which is general for it and other things. And 'tahrir' sometimes means expressing meaning in writing, just as 'taqrir' is expressing it in speech. Since there is not much benefit, the commentator did not address it. Then the preposition 'fi' is metaphorical, likening universal inclusion to container inclusion, using 'fi' which is placed for the second [type].
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This long set of notes discusses the interpretation of the phrase 'غاية تهذيب الكلام' (the utmost refinement of speech) that appears in the preface. The author (the original matn writer) said something like "هذا الكتاب غاية تهذيب الكلام" (this book is the utmost refinement of speech). The commentator (المحشى) found the literal meaning problematic because the meaning cannot be carried on the entity (العين) — that is, the physical book cannot literally be a mental concept like 'refinement'. Therefore, several interpretations are proposed: (1) Hyperbole (مبالغة) — the book itself is called 'refinement' as a metaphor, similar to saying 'Zaid is justice'. (2) Deletion — the original sentence had a deleted agent or modifier. Several possible deletions: 'this book is purified (مهذب) with a purification that is the utmost refinement', where the absolute object and its adjective replace the agent; or 'the composition of this book is the utmost refinement'; or 'this book is utmost refined speech' with the verbal noun in the sense of passive participle. (3) The note also discusses a possible objection: if 'refinement' means removing superfluities, but the book itself contains some superfluities, how can it be described as such? The commentator leaves that for later. Then additional notes (100-102) discuss the morphological form (whether it is active or passive participle) and the phenomenon of figurative deletion (مجاز الحذف) with examples from the Quran. The final note (103) defines 'tahrir' (writing) and distinguishes it from 'bayan' (clarification). These notes collectively show the careful logical and linguistic analysis typical of the hashiya tradition: every expression in the original must be shown to be meaningful and consistent with reality.

Key Terms
التهذيب (al-tahdhib): Refinement, purifying speech from defects and superfluities.
المبالغة (al-mubalagha): Hyperbole; a figurative expression where the thing itself is called the attribute to emphasize intensity.
مجاز الحذف (majaz al-hadhf): Figurative speech that involves deletion of a word, transferring the meaning to another word.
التحرير (al-tahrir): Expression free from verbiage and superfluities; also used for written expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which example from the Quran does the commentator use to illustrate 'مجاز الحذف' (figurative deletion)?
A) 'And your Lord came' (و جاء ربك)
B) 'And ask the village' (و اسئل القرية)
C) 'Nothing is like Him' (ليس كمثله شيء)
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Understanding the necessity of hyperbole and figurative deletion is crucial for correctly interpreting classical Islamic texts that might otherwise appear logically inconsistent.

Resolving Apparent Contradictions in Definitions: Ghāyat Tahdhīb
Resolving apparent flaw in calling a book 'the utmost refinement' through multiple linguistic and rhetorical analyses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the linguistic and rhetorical methods (metaphor, deletion, hyperbole) that Islamic logicians use to resolve apparent contradictions in definitions.

Core Idea
A flawed literal meaning can be rescued through metaphor, deletion, or estimation.

When a definition seems to contradict reality—like calling a book with some redundancies 'the utmost refinement'—classical logicians do not discard the definition. Instead, they deploy a sophisticated toolkit from Arabic linguistics and rhetoric to reveal the intended meaning. In this passage, the commentator resolves just such a difficulty using hyperbole, deletion of implicit phrases, and grammatical reanalysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize the apparent contradiction: a book is called 'the utmost refinement' yet it contains redundancies. This is the initial flaw that the commentator addresses.
The literal predication (ḥaml) seems false because the meaning of refinement cannot be directly attributed to the physical book.
2
Extract the Commentator's Solutions
List the three main approaches: hyperbole (mubālagha), deletion (taqdīr al-ḥadhf), and reinterpreting the infinitive as a derived word (ism maf‘ūl or ism fā‘il). Each resolves the literal contradiction differently.
The commentator also mentions the Kufan view that infinitives are always interpreted as derived words, making the predication literal in a generic sense.
3
Apply the Concept of Metaphor of Deletion
Understand the Quranic examples given (e.g., 'And your Lord came' meaning His command, 'Ask the village' meaning its people). These illustrate deletion (majāz al-ḥadhf) where an implicit word is necessary for correct predication.
The commentator argues that this case falls under the first type of metaphor of deletion.
4
Evaluate Which Interpretation Is Most Plausible
Note that the commentator prefers the passive participle reading (ism maf‘ūl) but also allows the active participle. Consider the potential objection: if the book contains redundancies, can it still be 'utmost refinement'? This forces deeper analysis.
The commentator anticipates later criticisms and leaves room for alternative readings.

(98) His statement: 'The predication of this intended meaning from this statement is a correction of the author's statement, for its apparent meaning is corrupt because it is obvious that the meaning is not predicated upon the eye, as preceded in the explanation of the author's statement: 'He sent it as guidance; it is by guidance true.' Then he limited here to the predication being for hyperbole or estimation of the predicate, and he did not allow the infinitive to be in the sense of the passive participle as he allowed earlier it to be in the sense of the active participle, because the addition to the statement does not suit that, as is apparent. Unless it is of the type of addition of the adjective to the described, so reflect. And you have known previously another aspect for solving the problem in similar cases, so refer to it. (Muhammad Ali) (99) His statement: 'As for hyperbole' — since the author's statement is not connected apparently, for it is not correct to say that the book is the utmost refinement, i.e., the utmost polish, because the meaning is not predicated upon the eye in reality, so the commentator answered that this predication is either by way of metaphor, where he made the eye itself the meaning for hyperbole, as in 'Zayd is justice', so the predicate did not bear the pronoun. The Kufans said: the infinitive is always interpreted as a derived word, so the predication is in reality and generic, bearing the pronoun. Or based on the estimation that this book is refined, often the operator of the absolute object is deleted and the absolute object is put in its place, and it is possible that the estimation is: this book is refined with a refinement that is the utmost refinement, so the operator was deleted and the absolute object put in its place, then the absolute object was deleted and its adjective put in its place. Or that the authorship of this book is the utmost refinement of speech by deleting the genitive. Or that this book is the utmost refined speech by making the infinitive in the sense of the passive participle and the addition is of the type of 'jurd qatīfa'. And one may say that the refinement of speech is its purification and cleansing from defects and redundancies, so how is this book described with it while it contains some redundancies? As will be pointed to later. (Abd al-Rahim) (100) The most apparent is that it is on the form of the passive participle, and it is permissible with a stretch to read it on the form of the active participle as well. (Muhammad Ali) (101) So the estimation of the statement became: this statement is the utmost of refinement, so the lam was deleted and the idafa was substituted for it to specify the intent and to maintain rhyme, then the description of the demonstrative noun was dispensed with because the statement indicates it. (Muhammad Ali) (102) His statement: 'On the method of metaphor of deletion' — the author said in the commentary on al-Talkhis: Know that a word, just as it is described as metaphorical for its transfer from its original meaning, likewise it is described as metaphorical for its transfer from its original syntax to another by deletion of a word or addition of a word. The first is like His statement: 'And your Lord came' and 'Ask the village'; the second is like His statement: 'There is nothing like Him', i.e., the command of your Lord came, because the coming of the Lord is impossible, and ask the people of the village because the intended is asking the people of the village, even though God is able to make the walls speak; and 'there is nothing like Him' because the intended is negating that anything is like Him, not negating that anything is like His like.' End summarized. And our case is of the first type, and the evidence for deletion is the invalidity of predication without it. (Muhammad Ali) (103) For 'tahrir' is the explanation free from redundancy and excess, unlike 'bayan' which is general for it and other than it, and sometimes tahrir is applied to expressing the meaning in writing, just as 'taqrir' is its expression in speech; and since it does not have much benefit, the commentator did not attend to it. Then the container (zarfiyya) is metaphorical, likening general inclusion to container inclusion, and the borrowing of 'fi' used for the second is metaphorical.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a phrase from the matn, likely 'ghāyat tahdhīb' (the utmost refinement). The commentator begins by noting that the apparent meaning is problematic because it literally predicates the meaning of refinement upon the physical book, which is not true. He then offers multiple linguistic and rhetorical solutions to resolve this. The first solution (98) is to interpret the predication as hyperbolic (mubālagha) or to estimate a deleted predicate, rather than taking the infinitive as a passive participle. In (99), the commentator expands on the hyperbolic interpretation, citing the example 'Zayd is justice' (Zaydun 'adlun) from Arabic rhetoric, where the attribute is metaphorically identified with the essence. He also mentions the Kufan school's view that infinitives are always interpreted as derived words, making the predication literal in a generic sense. Alternative estimations are given: deleting the operator of the absolute object and raising the object in its place, or deleting the absolute object and its adjective. Another possibility is deleting the genitive (mudāf) so that 'authorship of the book' is the subject. A potential objection is raised: if the book contains redundancies, how can it be described as the utmost refinement? This anticipates later criticisms. In (100), the commentator states that the most apparent reading is as a passive participle (ism maf'ūl), though the active participle is also possible. In (101), a different estimation is proposed, involving deletion of the lām of specification and substitution with iḍāfa. In (102), the concept of 'metaphor of deletion' (majāz al-ḥadhf) is introduced, supported by Quranic examples: 'Wa jā'a rabbuka' (And your Lord came) meaning the command of your Lord came, and 'Is'al al-qaryah' (Ask the village) meaning ask the people of the village. These illustrate deletion for rhetorical effect. The commentator asserts that this case falls under the first type (deletion), evidenced by the fact that predication fails without such deletion. Finally, in (103), he distinguishes tahrīr (editing/refinement) from bayān (explanation), noting that tahrīr specifically excludes redundancy, and that the commentator did not dwell on it. The passage showcases the rigorous linguistic analysis typical of Islamic logic commentaries, using tools from Arabic grammar, rhetoric, and exegesis to resolve apparent contradictions.

Analyzing the Agent in Conjunctive Constructions
Author discusses different views on the grammatical agent in conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the different views on the grammatical agent in a conjunctive structure as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
The agent of the conjoined word is the original agent acting through the conjunction.

In this passage, the commentator discusses a key grammatical point: what exactly is the 'āmil (governing agent) for a word linked by a conjunction? The matn's apparent view is that it is the same agent that governs the first clause, operating through the conjunction letter. The commentator then lists alternative positions and evaluates them. Understanding this debate clarifies how conjoined phrases are parsed in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical concept
The concept under discussion is the 'āmil (agent) that governs the case of the conjoined word (maʿṭūf) in a conjunctive structure (ʿaṭf). The passage presents multiple views on what this agent is.
2
List the different views
The passage mentions four views: (1) the majority view that the agent is the same agent of the first clause, acting through the conjunction letter; (2) some say the conjunction letter itself is the agent; (3) some say an implied agent exists after the conjunction; (4) some say if the agent were the followed word (matbūʿ), they would have evidence.
3
Determine the author's preferred view
The commentator states that the majority view is 'correct' (ṣaḥīḥ). He also notes that the fourth view is not explicitly endorsed but mentions it as a hypothetical that would have evidence.
4
Examine the additional note on conjoining to 'al-taḥrīr'
The commentator mentions an alternative interpretation by al-Dawwani that the word might be conjoined to 'al-taḥrīr' (editing). He evaluates this as closer in wording but not more appropriate in meaning, and therefore the original commentator did not address it.
This illustrates how grammatical analysis considers both linguistic form and semantic appropriateness.

(۱۰۷) الظاهر من كلامه ان العامل فى المعطوف هو عامل المعطوف عليه بواسطة الحرف كما عليه الجمهور وهو الصحيح. وذهب بعضهم الى ان العامل هو الحرف وقال بعضهم : ان العامل مقدر بعد العاطف وقال بعضهم لوقيل العامل فى التابع هو المتبوع لكان لهم .شواهد و يحتمل ان يكون معطوفاً على التحرير (كما قال به الفاضل الدوانى والمعنى هذا غاية تهذيب الكلام فى تقريب المقاصد ای : سوق الدليل على وجه يستلزم المطلوب. هكذا قيل. وفيه انه و ان كان بحسب اللفظ اقرب الا أنه ليس بحسب المعنى أنسب كما لا يخفى على من له فكر اصوب وذهن ارهب ولذا لم يتعرض له المحشى. (عبد الرحيم)
(107) It is apparent from his words that the agent in the conjoined (maʿṭūf) is the agent of the conjoined-upon (maʿṭūf ʿalayhi) through the letter (ḥarf), as is the view of the majority, and it is correct. Some went to the view that the agent is the letter itself. Some said that the agent is implied after the conjunction. And some said, 'If it were said that the agent in the follower is the followed, they would have evidence.' It is possible that it is conjoined to 'al-taḥrīr' as al-Fadil al-Dawwani said, and the meaning is: 'This is the ultimate refinement of speech in bringing goals near,' i.e., presenting evidence in a way that entails the required conclusion. Thus it is said. There is in it that although it is closer in wording, it is not more appropriate in meaning, as is not hidden from one with a sound mind and a sharp intellect, and therefore the commentator did not address it. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on a grammatical point concerning the agent (ʿāmil) in a conjunctive structure (ʿaṭf). The author (likely Abd al-Rahim) explains the apparent view from the matn: the agent of the conjoined word (maʿṭūf) is the same agent that acts on the conjoined-upon (maʿṭūf ʿalayhi), through the conjunction letter. This is the majority view and considered correct. He then mentions alternative views: some say the conjunction letter itself is the agent; some say an implied agent exists after the conjunction; and some say that if the agent were the followed (matbūʿ), they would have evidence. He also suggests that the word might be conjoined to 'al-taḥrīr' (editing), following al-Dawwani. The meaning of the phrase is about presenting evidence in a way that leads to the conclusion. The commentator notes that this interpretation is closer in wording but not more appropriate in meaning, and thus the original commentator did not address it.
